Preface
This book serves as reference class notes for Part A of the course MATH60610A at HEC Montréal.
I thank my students and Geneviève Gauthier for their comments and suggestions. All comments
and feedback are welcome. The usual copyrights apply.
4
Introduction
Modern quantitative finance requires several skills to perform cutting-edge asset allocation and
risk management. Thus, it is key to have a broad view of available methods in machine learning
that can be used in these contexts and understand their strengths and weaknesses. This is what
is proposed in the lecture notes Machine Learning Applied to Financial Data . Moreover, as
programming skills are essential in the field, especially in the context of these methods, we will
spend significant time coding in the R programming language. We will implement the various
techniques from scratch and using R packages. Real-life portfolio allocation problems will be
used to illustrate the different methods.
Outline
Learning objectives
At the end of this course, the learner should be able to:
– List and explain machine learning methods used in quantitative finance.
– Choose and apply machine learning methods to specific asset allocation problems.
– Develop and use R code to implement these methods.
– Share knowledge and ideas effectively in teams and through teamwork.
Why R?
R has been the lingua franca in statistical computing for several decades. It is a dinosaur but
is stable and mature. The R community is vast, and you will find plenty of forums with help,
like in Stack Overflow or R-F AQand RForge. You will find also help on RStudio community.
The massive advantage of R is the set of packages it offers. You have thousands of libraries of
functions that are available on CRAN. Also, you can look at packages for specific tasks on the
CRAN Task Views . In particular, look at the Finance Task View .
You cannot avoid R as a quantitative analyst working in the industry. Python has become a
competitor, especially in the Machine Learning community, but the Data Science community in
R is strong. Have a look at the book by Hadley Wickham , for instance, R for Data Science .
To have an overview of the pros. and cons. of R vs. Python, you can read the blog by Norm
Matloff. This is a great starting point for debating!

1 Introduction to R for financial applications
The current chapter is divided into four sections. Section 1.1 introduces R and RStudio.
Section 1.2 discusses financial packages that will be used throughout the course. Section 1.3
briefly introduces a few commonly used quantities in technical analysis. Finally, Section 1.4
discusses financial data and how they differ from other data typically encountered in Machine
Learning.
Learning objectives
At the end of this chapter, a learner should:
– Be familiar with RStudio functionalities.
– Load, transform, and manipulate financial datasets in R.
– Be accustomed to a few financial packages that can be used to measure performance,
carry out technical analysis, etc.

The material for this section is covered in detail in the course Statistical Methods for Financial

omit it to keep the text concise throughout the book (package::function()).
1.2 Financial packages
A collection of packages relevant to finance can be found in CRAN Task View: Empirical Finance .
Among them, the following will be used throughout the course (downloading data, transforming
data, portfolio evaluation, etc.):
• quantmod (Ryan and Ulrich , 2025) can be used to download data directly from sources
such as Yahoo Finance, Google Finance, and the Federal Reserve Economic Data (FRED).
It also contains plotting functions to plot securities and a set of technical indicators.
7
1 Introduction to R for financial applications
• PerformanceAnalytics (Peterson and Carl , 2024) contains several very nice functions
useful to compute the performance of a stock or a portfolio.
• TTR (Ulrich, 2023) is a package with functions and data to construct technical trading
rules with R.
1.3 Technical analysis
Technical analysis is a method of evaluating securities by analyzing the statistics generated by
market activity. It is based on three assumptions:
• The market discounts everything.
• Price moves in trends.
• History tends to repeat itself.
Technicians believe that all the information they need about a stock can be found in its charts.
Technical analysts take a short-term approach to analyzing the market. However, short-term
may refer to intraday, daily, weekly, or even monthly horizon.
A few terms frequently encountered in technical analysis are:
• Trend is the general direction in which a security (stock price) is going (e.g., up, down).
• Volume is the amount of shares or contracts traded over a given period.
• Momentum is the rate of change in the price or volume of a security.
Below are a few quantities commonly used for technical analysis.
We focus on price-based indicators in what follows to simplify the exposition. However,
many other indicators could be used in practice: macro-based variables, intra-day trades,
option-based measures such as implied volatility or implied skewness.
We advise starting building features with a few indicators that you understand. It is indeed
important to determine ex-ante what type of signal you want to capture. Later, you can
easily extend the set of features with dozens or hundreds of features and determine which
ones are important/selected by the model, and understand why.
1.3.1 Moving averages
A moving average is the average price of a stock over a given amount of time. Using moving
averages allows us to smooth out the price movement in the plot of a stock’s price. Traders can
more easily identify trends and price direction once the fluctuations are removed.
There exist multiple types of moving averages:
8
1 Introduction to R for financial applications
Simple moving average (SMA)
SMA is the sum of all past stock prices over a time period divided by the number of prices used
in the calculation. This is the most common method used to calculate the moving average of
prices. The 𝑁-day moving average at time 𝑡 is:
𝑆𝑀 𝐴𝑡(𝑁 ) =1
𝑁
𝑁−1
∑
𝑘=0
𝑃𝑡−𝑘 . (1.1)
Exponential moving average (EMA)
EMA moving average uses a smoothing factor to place a higher weight on recent data points.
It is important to remember that the exponential moving average is more responsive to new
information relative to the simple moving average. The exponential moving average at time 𝑡
with parameter 𝛼 = 2/(𝑁 + 1)is computed as follows:
𝐸𝑀 𝐴𝑡(𝑁 ) = 𝛼𝑃𝑡 + (1 − 𝛼)𝐸𝑀 𝐴𝑡−1(𝑁 ) . (1.2)
Here 𝛼 is a weight factor that determines how much weight to give to the most recent data
point (the higher the 𝛼, the higher the weight of the most recent data point in computing the
average).
Moving averages can be used to identify whether a stock is moving in an uptrend or a downtrend.
We will use them as numeric data that may help forecast the returns (features).
First we download Apple stock data from Yahoo Finance using getSymbols() from quantmod
from 2000 to 2019. We then compute the SMA and EMA using the functions SMA() and EMA().
library("quantmod")
library("xts")
# Set the system timezone to UTC (Coordinated Universal Time).
Sys.setenv(TZ = "UTC")
# ?quantmod::getSymbols
aapl <- getSymbols(Symbols = "AAPL",
src = "yahoo",
auto.assign = FALSE,
from = "2000-01-01",
to = "2019-12-31")
# Compute the simple moving average of the closing price (past 20 prices)
# ?TTR::SMA
sma <- SMA(Cl(aapl), n = 20)
round(tail(sma), digits = 2)
## SMA
## 2019-12-20 67.52
## 2019-12-23 67.80
## 2019-12-24 68.02
9
1 Introduction to R for financial applications
## 2019-12-26 68.34
## 2019-12-27 68.61
## 2019-12-30 68.92
# Do the same for the exponential moving average
# ?TTR::EMA
ema <- EMA(Cl(aapl), n = 20)
round(tail(ema), digits = 2)
## EMA
## 2019-12-20 67.81
## 2019-12-23 68.11
## 2019-12-24 68.39
## 2019-12-26 68.78
## 2019-12-27 69.13
## 2019-12-30 69.49
We then plot the 20-day SMA (red) and 20-day EMA (blue) for the first half of 2018 using
chartSeries() from quantmod.
# ?quantmod::chartSeries
chartSeries(aapl,
type = "candlesticks",
subset = "2018-01-01::2018-07-01",
theme = chartTheme("white"),
TA = c(addSMA(n = 20), addEMA(n = 20)))

1.3.2 Indicators and oscillators
Indicators are computations made on the price or the volume of a stock that help quantify
money flow, trends, volatility, and momentum, among others. Indicators are used as a secondary
measure of the actual price movements and provide more information to the analysis of securities.
Indicators are used in two main ways:
• To confirm price movement and the quality of chart patterns.
• To form buy and sell signals.
We distinguish two main types of indicators: leading and lagging. A leading indicator precedes
price movements, giving them a predictive quality. A lagging indicator is a confirmation tool as
it follows price movements.
The two main ways indicators are used to form buy and sell signals in technical analysis is via
crossovers and divergence.
Crossovers occur when either the price moves through the moving average, or when two different
moving averages cross over each other.
Divergence happens when the price trend and the indicator trend are moving in the opposite
direction. This indicates that the direction of the price trend is weakening.
Here are a few indicators and oscillators that we will use:
11
1 Introduction to R for financial applications
Average directional index (ADX)
ADX is a trend indicator. It is used to measure the strength of a current trend. It helps identify
the momentum behind trends. The ADX is a combination of two price movement measures:
the positive directional indicator (+DI) and the negative directional indicator (-DI). The ADX
measures the strength of a trend but not the direction. The +DI measures the strength of the
upward trend, while the -DI measures the strength of the downward trend.
We compute the ADX for Apple using the function ADX from TTR and plot its values for the
first half of 2018. The lower pane contains the ADX line (in blue), the +DI (green) and -DI (red)
lines. The function OHLC(x) extracts Open, High, Low, and Close prices from the x object.
adx <- round(ADX(OHLC(aapl), maType = "SMA"), digits = 2)
tail(adx)
## DIp DIn DX ADX
## 2019-12-20 60.39 16.07 57.96 39.76
## 2019-12-23 57.26 15.24 57.96 43.20
## 2019-12-24 61.51 13.58 63.84 46.47
## 2019-12-26 58.83 12.91 64.01 49.25
## 2019-12-27 64.79 10.95 71.07 51.93
## 2019-12-30 62.65 13.74 64.03 53.74
chartSeries(aapl,
type = "candlesticks",
subset = "2018-01-01::2018-07-01",
theme = chartTheme("white"),
TA = c(addADX()))

Aroon indicator (Aroon)
Aroon is a trending indicator used to measure whether a stock is in an uptrend or downtrend
and the magnitude of that trend. The indicator is also used to predict when a new trend is
beginning. The indicator is comprised of two lines, an “Aroon up” line and an “Aroon down”
line.
The Aroon up line measures the amount of time it has been since the highest price during the
time period. The Aroon down line measures the amount of time since the lowest price during
the time period.
The number of periods that are used in the calculation is dependent on the time frame that the
user wants to analyze.
We compute the Aroon indicator for Apple using the function aaron() and plot its values for
the first half of the year 2018 using two periods.
# ?TTR::aroon
# ?quantmod::Hi/Lo
my_aroon <- round(aroon(cbind(Hi(aapl), Lo(aapl))), digits = 2)
tail(my_aroon)
## aroonUp aroonDn oscillator
## 2019-12-20 100 35 65
## 2019-12-23 100 30 70
## 2019-12-24 100 25 75
## 2019-12-26 100 20 80
13
1 Introduction to R for financial applications
## 2019-12-27 100 15 85
## 2019-12-30 95 10 85
chartSeries(aapl,
type = "candlesticks",
subset = "2018-01-01::2018-07-01",
theme = chartTheme("white"),
TA = c(addAroon()))

Aroon oscillator
It is an extension of the Aroon. It plots the difference between the Aroon up and Aroon down
lines by subtracting the two lines. This line is then plotted in the range of -100 and 100.
The centerline at zero in the oscillator is considered to be a major signal line determining the
trend. The higher the value of the oscillator from the centerline point, the more upward strength
there is in the stock; The lower the oscillator’s value is from the centerline, the more downward
pressure.
A trend reversal is signaled when the oscillator crosses through the centerline. For example, a
downward trend is confirmed when the oscillator goes from positive to negative. Divergence is
also used in the oscillator to predict trend reversals. A reversal warning is formed when the
oscillator and the price trend are moving in an opposite direction.
14
1 Introduction to R for financial applications
chartSeries(aapl,
type = "candlesticks",
subset = "2018-01-01::2018-07-01",
theme = chartTheme("white"),
TA = c(addAroonOsc()))

Moving average convergence divergence (MACD)
MACD is one of the most well-known and used indicators in technical analysis. This indicator is
computed from two exponential moving averages, which help us measure momentum in the stock.
The MACD is the difference between these two moving averages plotted against a centerline.
The centerline is the point at which the two moving averages are equal. The idea behind this
momentum indicator is to measure short-term momentum compared to longer-term momentum
to help signal the current direction of momentum.
𝑀 𝐴𝐶𝐷 = 𝐸𝑀 𝐴𝑡(𝑁 ) − 𝐸𝑀 𝐴𝑡(𝑀 ), 𝑁 << 𝑀 ,
When the MACD is positive, it signals that the shorter-term moving average is above the
longer-term moving average and suggests upward momentum. A negative MACD signals that
the shorter term is below the longer and suggest downward momentum. When the MACD line
crosses over the centerline, it signals a crossing in the moving averages. It is typical to use the
26-day and 12-day exponential moving averages in the computation of the MACD.
15
1 Introduction to R for financial applications
macd <- MACD(Cl(aapl), nFast = 12, nSlow = 26)[,"signal"]
tail(macd)
## signal
## 2019-12-20 2.148489
## 2019-12-23 2.198160
## 2019-12-24 2.247822
## 2019-12-26 2.321734
## 2019-12-27 2.400431
## 2019-12-30 2.481780
chartSeries(aapl,
type = "candlesticks",
subset = "2018-01-01::2018-07-01",
theme = chartTheme("white"),
TA = c(addMACD()))

Relative strength index (RSI)
RSI is another commonly used momentum indicator in technical analysis. RSI allows us to detect
overbought and oversold conditions in a stock. The indicator is plotted in a range between 0
and 100. A value above 70 is used to suggest that a stock is overbought, while a value below 30
is used to suggest that it is oversold.
16
1 Introduction to R for financial applications
This indicator helps traders to identify whether a stock price has been unreasonably pushed to
its current level and whether a reversal may be on the way. Using 𝑛 = 14 trading periods to
compute the RSI is typical, but the value can be adjusted depending on the need.
rsi <- round(RSI(Cl(aapl), maType = "EMA", n = 14), digits = 2)
tail(rsi)
## rsi
## 2019-12-20 72.53
## 2019-12-23 79.96
## 2019-12-24 80.32
## 2019-12-26 86.31
## 2019-12-27 85.72
## 2019-12-30 87.28
chartSeries(aapl,
type = "candlesticks",
subset = "2018-01-01::2018-07-01",
theme = chartTheme("white"),
TA = c(addRSI()))

Chaikin money flow (CMF)
CMF is a technical indicator that measures money flow volume over a set period of time. Money
flow volume is a metric used to measure the buying and selling pressure of a security for a single
period of time.
17
1 Introduction to R for financial applications
The CMF then sums money flow volume over a user-defined period. Any period can be used but
the typical settings are 20 or 21 days. The CMF value fluctuates between 1 and -1. It is used
as a way to further quantify changes in buying and selling pressure, and to anticipate future
changes and, therefore, trading opportunities.
cmf <- round(CMF(OHLC(aapl), Vo(aapl)), digits = 2)
tail(cmf)
## [,1]
## 2019-12-20 7.13
## 2019-12-23 6.86
## 2019-12-24 7.36
## 2019-12-26 5.39
## 2019-12-27 5.36
## 2019-12-30 5.32
chartSeries(aapl,
type = "candlesticks",
subset = "2018-01-01::2018-07-01",
theme = chartTheme("white"),
TA = c(addCMF()))

Stochastic oscillator/stochastic momentum index (SMI)
The stochastic oscillator is one of the most used momentum indicators in technical analysis. The
idea behind this indicator is that in an uptrend, the price should be closing near the highs of
18
1 Introduction to R for financial applications
the trading range, signaling upward momentum in the stock. In downtrends, the price should
be closing near the lows of the trading range, signaling downward momentum.
The stochastic oscillator is plotted within a range of 0 and 100 and signals overbought conditions
above 80 and oversold conditions below 20. The stochastic oscillator contains two lines. The
first line, the %K quantifies the idea of momentum behind the oscillator. The second line, the
%D, is a moving average of the %K. The %D line is preferred to %K as it produces better
signals empirically. The stochastic oscillator generally uses the past 14 trading periods in its
calculation, but the period can be changed depending on the needs.
The SMI relates the close to the midpoint of the high/low range instead of the high/low range.
smi <- round(SMI(HLC(aapl)), digits = 2)
tail(smi)

1.4 Financial data
Data is at the core of statistical/machine learning. Most statistical/machine learning methods
usually assume that the data are independent and identically distributed (iid) .
However, this assumption is not typically true in finance. It is easy, for example, to see that the
price of a stock on a day should be close to the price on a preceding day. This is shown below
by plotting the prices of Apple stock for the years 2000-2019.
aapl_prices <- aapl[, 4] # Pick close prices

2 Statistical/machine learning and model
accuracy in finance
In this chapter, we introduce the concept of statistical/machine learning and model accuracy.
We start in Section 2.1 by introducing the concept of statistical/machine learning. We discuss
how and why to estimate statistical/machine models, the trade-off between accuracy and inter-
pretability, the concept of supervised and unsupervised learning, regression and classification.
In Section 2.2, we assess model accuracy through quality of fit, introduce and discuss the bias-
variance trade-off in both the regression and classification context.
Learning objectives
At the end of this chapter, a learner should:
– Understand the definition of statistical/machine learning.
– Be able to differentiate between parametric versus non-parametric methods, super-
vised versus unsupervised learning problems, and regression versus classification prob-
lems.
– Be able to assess the quality of fit of a model for regression and classification.
– Understand the bias-variance trade-off.
Resources
This chapter is based on the following reference:
• An Introduction to Statistical Learning, Chapter 2 by Gareth James, Daniela Witten,
Trevor Hastie, and Robert Tibshirani
2.1 Statistical/machine learning in finance
Imagine you are a trader, and you would like to use information such as past weekly prices and
a few technical indicators (e.g., ATR, SMI) to predict the returns of a set of stocks in a week.
Here, the past weekly prices, ATR, and SMI are input variables . They are also referred to as
predictors, independent variables, or features . We will denote them by 𝑋. So 𝑋1 could represent
the past prices, 𝑋2 the ATR, and 𝑋3 the SMI.
The returns of the stocks are the output variables also known as response or dependent variables.
We will denote them by 𝑌 .
27
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
The more general problem could be stated as follows: Assume we have 𝑋1, 𝑋2, … , 𝑋𝑝 different
predictors and that there is a relationship between 𝑋 = (𝑋1, 𝑋2, … , 𝑋𝑝)′ and 𝑌 . We can write
the relationship in the general form:
𝑌 = 𝑓(𝑋) + 𝜖 . (2.1)
Here 𝑓 is some fixed but unknown function of 𝑋1, 𝑋2, … , 𝑋𝑝, and 𝜖 is a random error term,
which is independent of 𝑋 and has mean zero. In this formulation, 𝑓 represents the systematic
information that 𝑋 provides about 𝑌 .
The assumption of the independence of the error terms from the predictors is very impor-
tant but, unfortunately often not satisfied with financial time series data!
We shall discuss in the subsequent chapters the assumptions each machine learning model makes
about the data.
Statistical/machine learning refers to a set of approaches for estimating the function 𝑓. In this
chapter, we will discuss some of the key theoretical concepts that arise in estimating 𝑓 and tools
for evaluating the estimates obtained.
2.1.1 Why estimate 𝑓?
There are typically two reasons we would like to estimate 𝑓: prediction and inference.
Prediction
For the prediction problem, we are in a situation where the predictors 𝑋 are available, but the
response 𝑌 cannot be easily found. Here, since the error term averages to zero (by assumption),
we can predict 𝑌 using:
̂𝑌 = ̂𝑓(𝑋) , (2.2)
where ̂𝑓 is our estimate for 𝑓 and ̂𝑌 is the resulting prediction for 𝑌 . In this setting, ̂𝑓 is often
treated as a black box . This means we are usually not interested in the exact form of ̂𝑓, as long
as it yields accurate predictions for 𝑌 .
The accuracy of ̂𝑌 as a prediction for 𝑌 depends on two quantities, the reducible error and the
irreducible error. In general, ̂𝑓 will not be a perfect estimate for 𝑓, and this inaccuracy will
introduce some errors. This error is reducible since we could theoretically improve the accuracy
of ̂𝑓 by using the most appropriate statistical/machine learning technique to estimate 𝑓.
However, even if it were possible to form a perfect estimate for 𝑓 so that our estimated response
took the form ̂𝑌 = 𝑓(𝑋), our prediction would still have some error in it. This is because 𝑌 is
also a function of 𝜖, which, by definition, cannot be predicted using 𝑋. Therefore, variability
associated with 𝜖 also affects the accuracy of our predictions. This is known as the irreducible
error, because no matter how well we estimate 𝑓, we cannot reduce the error introduced by 𝜖.
Why is the irreducible error larger than zero?
28
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
• The quantity 𝜖 may contain unmeasured variables that are useful in predicting 𝑌 : it is
diﬀicult to determine all the variables that could affect the returns of a stock a week from
now, for example. Since we might miss some of those variables, 𝑓 cannot use them for
prediction.
• The quantity 𝜖 may also contain unmeasurable variations. For example, the return of a
stock in a given week will depend on the general state of the market, the state of the sector
to which the stock belongs, but also on the state of the firm (information about the firm).
Consider a given estimate ̂𝑓 and a set of predictors 𝑋, which yields the prediction ̂𝑌 = ̂𝑓(𝑋).
Assuming that both ̂𝑓 and 𝑋 are fixed, we have:
𝐸[(𝑌 − ̂𝑌 )2] = 𝐸 [(𝑓(𝑋) + 𝜖 −̂𝑓(𝑋))
2
]
= (𝑓(𝑋) −̂𝑓(𝑋))
2
⏟⏟ ⏟⏟⏟ ⏟⏟
Reducible
+ V[𝜖]⏟
Irreducible
, (2.3)
where 𝐸[(𝑌 − ̂𝑌 )2]represents the average, or expected value, of the squared difference between
the predicted and actual value of 𝑌 , and V [𝜖]represents the variance associated with the error
term 𝜖.
The main objective of this course is to learn techniques for estimating 𝑓 with the aim of mini-
mizing the reducible error. The irreducible error provides an upper bound on the accuracy of
our prediction for 𝑌 , however, as it cannot be reduced. In practice, the irreducible error is often
not known.
Inference
This problem is less relevant in portfolio and risk management and in general, in real-life financial
application. With inference, we are often interested in understanding the way 𝑌 is affected as
𝑋1, 𝑋2, … , 𝑋𝑝 change. In this situation, we wish to estimate 𝑓, but our goal is not necessarily
to make predictions for 𝑌 . We instead want to understand the relationship between 𝑋 and 𝑌 ,
or more specifically, understand how changes in 𝑋1, 𝑋2, … , 𝑋𝑝 affect 𝑌 .
Here, we cannot treat 𝑓 as a black box because we need to know its exact form. In this setting,
one may be interested in answering the following questions:
• Which predictors are associated with the response? Very often, only a small fraction of
the available predictors are actually necessary to predict 𝑌 . Finding the few important
predictors among a large set of possible variables can be extremely useful (remember,
garbage in, garbage out ).
• What is the relationship between the response and each predictor? Some predictors may
have a positive relationship with 𝑌 , meaning a positive variation of the predictor is asso-
ciated with increasing values of 𝑌 . Other predictors may have the opposite relationship.
Depending on how complex 𝑓 is, the relationship between the dependent variable and a
given input variable could be linked to the values of the other input variables.
• Can the relationship between 𝑌 and each predictor be adequately summarized using a linear
equation, or is the relationship more complicated? The majority of methods for estimating
𝑓 have a linear form. This assumption is reasonable in some situations. However, the true
relationship is usually more complicated. In this case, a linear model might not provide
an accurate representation of the relationship between the response and predictors.
29
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
An example of a prediction problem was mentioned earlier, which is to predict the returns for a
stock given past prices and a few technical indicators.
An inference problem would be, for example, to find if one of the predictors (e.g., ATR, SMI)
is really associated with the returns? Whether the association is positive or negative (i.e., is an
increase in ATR associated with an increase in the returns, or is the opposite true)? Whether
the relationship is linear or more complex?
Depending on whether our ultimate goal is prediction, inference, or a combination of the two,
different methods for estimating 𝑓 may be appropriate. For example, linear models allow for
relatively simple and interpretable inference, but their predictions might not be as accurate pre-
dictions as some other approaches. On the other hand, some of the highly non-linear approaches
that we will discuss in the subsequent chapters of these notes can provide more accurate predic-
tions for 𝑌 at the expense of interpretability, giving rise to models for which inference is more
challenging.
In this course, we are primarily interested in the prediction problems. This is actually what
computer scientists, machine learners, etc., do.
How to estimate 𝑓?
In the following chapters, we will study linear and non-linear approaches for estimating the
function 𝑓. Those approaches share specific characteristics that we shall explore in the current
section.
Let us assume that we have observed a set of 𝑛 different data points. We call these observations
training data because we will use them to train or teach our methods how to estimate 𝑓.
Let 𝑥𝑖𝑗 represent the value of the 𝑗th predictor, or input, for observation 𝑖, where 𝑖 = 1, 2, … , 𝑛
and 𝑗 = 1, 2, … , 𝑝. Correspondingly, let 𝑦𝑖 represent the response variable for the 𝑖th observation.
Then our training data consist of {(𝑥1, 𝑦1), (𝑥2, 𝑦2), … , (𝑥𝑛, 𝑦𝑛)}where 𝑥𝑖 = (𝑥𝑖1, 𝑥𝑖2, … , 𝑥𝑖𝑝)′.
We aim to apply a statistical/machine learning method to the training data to estimate the
unknown function 𝑓. In other words, we want to find a function ̂𝑓 such that 𝑌 ≈ ̂𝑓(𝑋)for any
observation (𝑋, 𝑌 ). Broadly speaking, most statistical/machine learning methods for this task
can be characterized as either parametric or non-parametric.
Parametric methods
Parametric methods are a two-step model-based approach:
• In the first step, we make an assumption about the functional form, or shape, of 𝑓. One
simple assumption is that 𝑓 is linear in 𝑋:
𝑓(𝑋) = 𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋1 + 𝛽2𝑋2 + … + 𝛽𝑝𝑋𝑝 .
This is a linear model, which will be discussed extensively in the next chapter. Once we have
assumed that 𝑓 is linear, the problem of estimating 𝑓 is greatly simplified. Instead of having to
estimate an entirely arbitrary 𝑝-dimensional function 𝑓(𝑋), we only need to estimate the 𝑝 + 1
coeﬀicients 𝛽0, 𝛽1, … , 𝛽𝑝.
30
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
• The second step is determining a procedure that uses the training data to fit or train the
model. For the linear model, this is equivalent to estimating 𝛽0, 𝛽1, … , 𝛽𝑝. That is, we
want to find the values of these parameters such that:
𝑓(𝑋) ≈ 𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋1 + 𝛽2𝑋2 + … + 𝛽𝑝𝑋𝑝 .
The most common approach to fit the equation above is known as (ordinary) least-squares, which
we shall see in the next chapter. Other methods exist to find the parameters 𝛽𝑖, and we shall
learn a few more when dealing with cross-validation.
The model-based approach just described is referred to as parametric; It reduces the problem
of estimating 𝑓 to estimating a set of parameters. Assuming a parametric form for 𝑓 simplifies
the problem of estimating 𝑓 because it is generally much easier to estimate a set of parameters,
such as 𝛽0, 𝛽1, … , 𝛽𝑝 in the linear model than it is to fit an entirely arbitrary function 𝑓.
The potential disadvantage of a parametric approach is that the model we choose will usually
not match the true unknown form of 𝑓. If the chosen model is too far from the true 𝑓, then our
estimate will be poor.
One way to solve this problem is to choose flexible models that can fit many different functional
forms for 𝑓. However, fitting a more flexible model requires estimating more parameters. These
more complex models can lead to a phenomenon known as 𝑜𝑣𝑒𝑟𝑓𝑖𝑡𝑡𝑖𝑛𝑔the data, which essentially
means the models follow the errors or 𝑛𝑜𝑖𝑠𝑒too closely. Overfitting will be discussed throughout
this course.
Non-parametric methods
Non-parametric methods do not make explicit assumptions about the functional form of 𝑓. They
instead look for an estimate of 𝑓 that gets as close to the data points as possible without being
too rough or wiggly.
The major advantage of the non-parametric methods over parametric methods is
that by avoiding the assumption of a particular functional form for 𝑓, they could accurately fit
a wider range of possible shapes for 𝑓. A parametric method carries the possibility that the
functional form used to estimate 𝑓 is very different from the true 𝑓, in which case the resulting
model will not fit the data well. In contrast, non-parametric approaches completely avoid this
danger because no assumption about the form of 𝑓 is made.
But non-parametric approaches do suffer from a major disadvantage: they do not reduce the
problem of estimating 𝑓 to a small number of parameters and, hence, a very large number of
observations (far more than usually needed for a parametric approach) is required to obtain an
accurate estimate for 𝑓.
An example of a non-parametric approach is the K-nearest neighbors (KNN).
2.1.2 The trade-off between prediction accuracy and model interpretability
We will see several methods in this course. Some are less flexible or more restrictive, meaning
they can only produce a relatively small range of shapes to estimate 𝑓. Linear regression is
inflexible because it can only generate linear functions.
Other methods, such as the trees or artificial neural networks (ANN) , are considerably more
flexible because they can generate a much wider range of possible shapes to estimate 𝑓.
31
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
A reasonable question to ask is: Why choose a more restrictive method instead of a very flexible
one? Multiple reasons could lead to the choice of a more restrictive method over a more flexible
model:
• If we are mainly interested in inference, then restrictive models are much more inter-
pretable. For example, when inference is the goal, the linear model may be a good choice
since it will be quite easy to understand the relationship between 𝑌 and 𝑋1, 𝑋2, … , 𝑋𝑝.
• In contrast, very flexible approaches, such as neural nets can lead to such complicated esti-
mates of 𝑓 that it is diﬀicult to understand the relationship between individual predictors
and the response.
Figure. A representation of the trade-off between flexibility and interpretability, using different
statistical learning methods. In general, as the flexibility of a method increases, its interpretabil-
ity decreases. Source: James et al. (2013).
The figure above provides an illustration of the trade-off between flexibility and interpretability
for some of the methods that we will cover. Least squares linear regression is relatively inflexible
but is quite interpretable. The lasso relies upon the linear model but uses an alternative fitting
procedure for estimating the coeﬀicients 𝛽0, 𝛽1, … , 𝛽𝑝. The procedure is more restrictive in
estimating the coeﬀicients and sets a number of them to exactly zero. Therefore, the lasso can
be seen as a less flexible approach than linear regression. It is also more interpretable than linear
regression because in the final model, the output variable will be related to a smaller subset of
the predictors, basically those with nonzero coeﬀicient estimates. Fully non-linear methods such
as bagging, boosting, and SVM with non-linear kernels are highly flexible approaches that are
harder to interpret. Finally, ANN are among the most highly non-linear and flexible methods.
In this section, we have shown that when inference is the goal, there are clear advantages to
using simple and relatively inflexible statistical learning methods. In some settings, however,
we are only interested in prediction and the interpretability of the predictive model is not of
interest.
For instance, if we seek to develop an algorithm to predict the price of a stock, our sole require-
ment for the algorithm is that it predicts accurately. Here, interpretability is not a concern. In
this setting, we might expect that using the most flexible model available will be best.
32
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
Surprisingly, this is not always the case! We will often obtain more accurate predictions using a
less flexible method. This phenomenon, which may seem counter-intuitive, has to do with the
potential for overfitting in highly flexible methods. We will discuss this very important concept
further throughout the course.
2.1.3 Supervised versus unsupervised learning
Most statistical/machine learning problems fall into one of two categories: supervised or unsu-
pervised. The examples so far all fall into the supervised learning domain. For each observation
of the predictor measurement(s) 𝑥𝑖, 𝑖 = 1, … , 𝑛, there is an associated response measurement 𝑦𝑖.
We wish to fit a model that relates the response to the predictors, with the aim of accurately pre-
dicting the response for future observations (prediction) or better understanding the relationship
between the response and the predictors (inference). Many classical statistical/machine learning
methods, such as linear regression and logistic regression, and more modern approaches, such
as bagging and support vector machine, operate in the supervised learning domain.
On the other hand, unsupervised learning describes the more challenging situation in which for
every observation 𝑖 = 1, … , 𝑛, we observe a vector of measurements 𝑥𝑖 but no associated response
𝑦𝑖. It is not possible to fit a linear regression model, for example, since there is no response
variable to predict. In this setting, we are, in some sense, working blind; the situation is referred
to as unsupervised because we lack a response variable that can supervise our analysis. What
sort of statistical analysis is possible? We can seek to understand the relationships between the
variables or between the observations.
One statistical learning tool we may use in this setting is cluster analysis, or clustering. The goal
of cluster analysis is to ascertain, based on 𝑥1, … , 𝑥𝑛, whether the observations fall into relatively
distinct groups. For example, to build a diversified portfolio from the S&P 500 components,
we could use clustering to split the index components into various groups and pick the best-
performing stock from each group.
A last paradigm not discussed in this course but present in machine learning is reinforcement
learning. Here, the model (agent) learns through experience (trial and error) to perform the
actions that would maximize a cumulative reward.
2.1.4 Regression versus classification problems
Variables can be characterized as either quantitative or qualitative (also known as categorical).
Quantitative variables take on numerical values. This includes the price of a stock, the returns,
various technical indicators associated with the stocks (ATR, SMI). In contrast, qualitative
variables take on values in one of K different classes, or categories. Examples of qualitative
variables include the direction of a stock’s returns ( + or -, up or down), and actions to take for
a given stock in a portfolio ( buy, sell, hold).
Problems with a quantitative response are known as regression problems, while those with a
qualitative response are called classification problems. The distinction is not always that clear-
cut, however. Least squares linear regression is used with a quantitative response, whereas
logistic regression is typically used with a qualitative (two-class or binary) response. It is,
therefore, often used as a classification method. But, the fact that it estimates class probabilities
could make it a regression method as well. Some statistical methods, such as K-nearest neighbors
and trees, can be used in the case of either quantitative or qualitative responses.
33
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
Statistical/machine learning methods are usually selected based on whether the response is
quantitative or qualitative; for example, we might use linear regression when the response is
quantitative and logistic regression when it is qualitative.
Whether the predictors are qualitative or quantitative is typically considered less important.
Most of the statistical learning methods discussed in these notes can be applied regardless of
the predictor variable type, provided that any qualitative predictors are properly coded before
the analysis is performed.
2.2 Model accuracy in finance
We will talk about a wide range of statistical/machine learning methods that go far beyond linear
regression. Why is it necessary to introduce so many different statistical learning approaches,
rather than just a single best method?
There is no free lunch in statistics: No single method dominates all others over all possible
data sets. On a given data set, one specific method may work best, but some other method
may work better on a similar but different data set.
Hence, it is important to decide for any given set of data which method produces the best
results. Selecting the best approach can be one of the most challenging parts of performing
statistical/machine learning in practice.
We now discuss some of the most important concepts in selecting a statistical/machine learning
procedure for a specific data set. Later, we will explain how the concepts presented here can be
applied in practice.
2.2.1 Measuring the quality of fit
To evaluate the performance of a statistical learning method on a given data set, we need some
way to measure how well its predictions match the observed data. In other words, we need to
quantify the extent to which the predicted response value for a given observation is close to the
actual response value for that observation.
In the regression setting, the most commonly used measure is the mean squared error (MSE),
given by:
𝑀 𝑆𝐸 = 1
𝑛
𝑛
∑
𝑖=1
(𝑦𝑖 − ̂𝑓(𝑥𝑖))2 , (2.4)
where ̂𝑓(𝑥𝑖)is the prediction that ̂𝑓 gives for the 𝑖th observation. The MSE will be small if
the predicted responses are very close to the true responses and will be large if, for some of the
observations, the predicted and true responses differ substantially.
The MSE is computed using the training data that was used to fit the model. It is, therefore,
more accurate to refer to it as the training MSE. In finance, we also often refer to it as in-sample
MSE.
In general, we do not really care how well the method works on the training data. Instead, our
interest lies in the accuracy of the predictions of our model when applied to previously unseen
test data. Why? Suppose that we are interested in developing an algorithm to predict a stock’s
34
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
return based on previous stock’s returns. We can train the method using the stock returns from
the past five years. But we do not care how well our method predicts last week’s stock return.
Instead, we care about how well it will predict tomorrow’s or next month’s returns.
Stated otherwise, suppose that we fit our statistical learning method on our training obser-
vations {(𝑥1, 𝑦1), (𝑥2, 𝑦2), … , (𝑥𝑛, 𝑦𝑛)}and we obtain the estimate ̂𝑓. We can then compute
̂𝑓(𝑥1), ̂𝑓(𝑥2), … , ̂𝑓(𝑥𝑛). If these are approximately equal to 𝑦1, 𝑦2, … , 𝑦𝑛, then the training MSE
given by Equation ( 2.4) is small. However, we are not interested in whether ̂ 𝑦𝑖 ≈ ̂𝑓(𝑥𝑖). Instead,
we want to know whether ̂𝑓(𝑥0)is approximately equal to 𝑦0 where (𝑥0, 𝑦0)is a previously un-
seen test observation not used to train the statistical learning method . We want to choose the
method that gives the lowest test MSE or out-of-sample MSE instead of the lowest training
MSE or in-sample MSE . In other words, if we had a large number of test observations, we could
compute:
Ave [(𝑦0 − ̂𝑓(𝑥0))
2
] , (2.5)
the average squared prediction error for these test observations (𝑥0, 𝑦0). We want to select the
model for which the average of the test MSE is as small as possible.
How can we select a method that minimizes the test MSE? In some cases, we may have a test
data set available, i.e., we may have access to a set of observations that were not used to train
the statistical learning method. We can then evaluate Equation ( 2.5) on the test observations,
and select the learning method for which the test MSE is smallest.
What if there are no test observations? In that case, one might think it would be best to simply
select a statistical/machine learning method that minimizes the training MSE given by Equation
(2.4). This seems like it might be a sensible approach since the training MSE and the test MSE
appear to be closely related.
Unfortunately, this strategy has a fundamental problem: there is no guarantee that the method
with the lowest training MSE will also have the lowest test MSE. This is because many methods
specifically estimate coeﬀicients to minimize the training set MSE. The training set MSE can
be quite small for these methods, but the test MSE is often much larger.
The figure below illustrates this phenomenon in a simple example. On the left-hand panel, we
have generated observations from Equation ( 2.1) with the true 𝑓 given by the black curve. The
orange, blue, and green curves illustrate three possible estimates for 𝑓 obtained using methods
with increasing levels of flexibility. The orange line is the linear regression fit, which is relatively
inflexible. The blue and green curves were produced using regression splines. We notice that as
the level of flexibility increases, the curves fit the observed data more closely. The green curve
is the most flexible and matches the data very well; however, we observe that it fits the true
𝑓 (shown in black) poorly because it is too wiggly. By adjusting the level of flexibility of the
spline, we can produce many different fits to this data.
We now move on to the right-hand panel. The grey curve displays the average training MSE
as a function of flexibility, or more formally, the degrees of freedom , for a number of spline
regressors. The degrees of freedom is a quantity that summarizes the flexibility of a curve. The
orange, blue, and green squares indicate the MSEs associated with the corresponding curves in
the left-hand panel. A more restricted and hence smoother curve has fewer degrees of freedom
than a wiggly curve—note, linear regression is at the most restrictive end, with two degrees of
freedom. The training MSE declines monotonically as flexibility increases. In this example, the
true 𝑓 is non-linear, and so the orange linear fit is not flexible enough to estimate 𝑓 well. The
35
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
green curve has the lowest training MSE of all three methods, since it corresponds to the most
flexible of the three curves fit in the left-hand panel.
Figure 2.1. Data simulated from 𝑓, shown in black. Three estimates of 𝑓 are shown: the linear
regression line (orange curve), and two smoothing spline fits (blue and green curves). Right:
Training MSE (grey curve), test MSE (red curve), and minimum possible test MSE over all
methods (dashed line). Squares represent the training and test MSEs for the three fits shown
in the left-hand pane. Source: James et al. (2013).
In this example, we know the true function 𝑓, so we can also compute the test MSE over a
large test set as a function of flexibility. In general, 𝑓 is unknown, so this will not be possible.
The test MSE is displayed using the red curve in the right-hand panel. As with the training
MSE, the test MSE initially declines as the level of flexibility increases. However, at some point,
the test MSE levels off and then starts to increase again. Consequently, the orange and green
curves both have high test MSE. The blue curve minimizes the test MSE, which should not be
surprising given that visually, it appears to estimate 𝑓 the best in the left-hand panel of the
figure. The horizontal dashed line indicates 𝑉 [𝜖], the irreducible error, which corresponds to the
lowest achievable test MSE among all possible methods. Hence, the spline regressor represented
by the blue curve is nearly optimal.
In the right-hand panel, as the flexibility of the statistical learning method increases, we observe
a monotone decrease in the training MSE and a U-shape in the test MSE. This is a fundamental
property of statistical/machine learning that holds regardless of the particular data set at hand
and regardless of the statistical method being used. As model flexibility increases, training
MSE will decrease, but the test MSE may not. When a given method yields a small training
MSE but a large test MSE, we are said to be overfitting the data. This happens because our
statistical/machine learning procedure is working too hard to find patterns in the training data
and maybe picking up some patterns that are just caused by random chance rather than by true
properties of the unknown function 𝑓. When we overfit the training data, the test MSE will be
very large because the supposed patterns that the method found in the training data simply don’t
exist in the test data. Regardless of whether overfitting has occurred, we almost always expect
the training MSE to be smaller than the test MSE because most statistical learning methods
36
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
either directly or indirectly seek to minimize the training MSE. Overfitting refers specifically to
the case in which a less flexible model would have yielded a smaller test MSE.
The figure below provides another example in which the true 𝑓 is approximately linear. Again,
we observe that the training MSE decreases monotonically as the model flexibility increases.
However, because the true 𝑓 is close to linear, the test MSE barely decreases at all before
starting to climb again. So, the orange least squares fit is substantially better than the highly
flexible green curve.
Figure 2.2. Details are as in Figure 2.1, using a different 𝑓 that is far from linear. In this setting,
linear regression provides a very good fit to the data. Source: James et al. (2013).
Finally, the figure below displays an example in which 𝑓 is highly non-linear. The training and
test MSE curves still exhibit the same general patterns, but now there is a rapid decrease in
both curves before the test MSE starts to increase slowly.
In practice, one can usually compute the training MSE with relative ease, but estimating the test
MSE is considerably more diﬀicult because usually no test data are available. As the previous
three examples illustrate, the flexibility level corresponding to the model with the minimal test
MSE can vary considerably among data sets. We will discuss later cross-validation, which is a
method for estimating the test MSE using the training data.
MSE is a statistical measure and might not be the most suitable metric to assess how well a
model is doing for quantitative finance applications. We will, for example, use backtesting
to assess our model performance in subsequent chapters!
2.2.2 The bias-variance trade-off
The Bias Variance Trade-Off, Mutual Information Machine Learning Fundamentals: Bias
and Variance, StatQuest
37
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
Figure 2.3. Details are as in Figure 2.1, using a different 𝑓 that is far from linear. In this setting,
linear regression provides a very poor fit to the data. Source: James et al. (2013).
The U-shape (a decrease followed by an increase) observed in the test MSE curves turns out to
be the result of two competing properties of statistical/machine learning methods. It is possible
to show that the expected test MSE, for a given value 𝑥0, can always be decomposed into the
sum of three fundamental quantities: the variance of ̂𝑓(𝑥0), the squared bias of ̂𝑓(𝑥0)and the
variance of the error terms 𝜖.

Here the notation 𝐸 [(𝑦0 − ̂𝑓(𝑥0))
2
] defines the expected test MSE, and refers to the average
test MSE that we would obtain if we repeatedly estimated 𝑓 using a large number of training
38
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
sets and tested each at 𝑥0. The overall expected test MSE can be computed by averaging
𝐸 [(𝑦0 − ̂𝑓(𝑥0))
2
] over all possible values of 𝑥0 in the test set.
Equation ( 2.6) tells us that to minimize the expected test error, we need to select a statisti-
cal/machine learning method that simultaneously achieves low variance and low bias .
Note that variance is inherently a non-negative quantity and squared bias is also non-negative.
Hence, the expected test MSE can never lie below 𝑉 [𝜖], the irreducible error from ( 2.4).
What do we mean by the variance and bias of a statistical/machine learning method? Variance
refers to the amount by which ̂𝑓 would change if we estimated it using a different training data
set. Since the training data are used to fit the statistical/machine learning method, different
training data sets will result in a different ̂𝑓. But ideally, the estimate for 𝑓 should not vary
too much between training sets. However, if a method has high variance, then small changes in
the training data can result in large changes in ̂𝑓. In general, more flexible statistical methods
have higher variance. Consider the green and red curves in Figure 2.2. The flexible green curve
follows the observations very closely. It has a high variance because changing any one of these
data points may cause the estimate ̂𝑓 to change considerably. In contrast, the red least squares
line is relatively inflexible and has low variance because moving any single observation will likely
cause only a small shift in the position of the line.
On the other hand, bias refers to the error introduced by approximating a real-life problem,
which may be extremely complicated, by a much simpler model. For example, linear regression
assumes a linear relationship between 𝑌 and 𝑋1, 𝑋2, … , 𝑋𝑝. It is unlikely that any real-life
problem has such a simple linear relationship, so performing linear regression will undoubtedly
result in some bias in the 𝑓. In Figure 2.4, the true 𝑓 is substantially non-linear, so no matter
how many training observations we are given, we cannot produce an accurate estimate using
linear regression. In other words, linear regression results in high bias in this example. However,
in Figure 2.3, the true 𝑓 is very close to linear, and so, given enough data, it should be possible
for linear regression to produce an accurate estimate. Generally, more flexible methods result
in less bias.
As a general rule, as we use more flexible methods, the variance will increase, and the bias
will decrease. The relative rate of change of these two quantities determines whether the test
MSE increases or decreases. As we increase the flexibility of a class of methods, the bias tends
to initially decrease faster than the variance increases. Consequently, the expected test MSE
declines. However, increasing flexibility has little impact on the bias at some point but starts to
increase the variance significantly. When this happens, the test MSE increases. Note that we
observed this pattern of decreasing test MSE followed by increasing test MSE in the right-hand
panels of Figures 2.2-2.4.
The three plots in Figure 2.5 illustrate Equation ( 2.6) for the examples in Figures 2.2-2.4. In
each case, the blue solid curve represents the squared bias for different levels of flexibility, while
the orange curve corresponds to the variance. The horizontal dashed line represents 𝑉 [𝜖], the
irreducible error. Finally, the red curve, corresponding to the test set MSE, is the sum of
these three quantities. In all three cases, the variance increases, and the bias decreases as the
method’s flexibility increases. However, the flexibility level corresponding to the optimal test
MSE differs considerably among the three data sets because the squared bias and variance
change at different rates in each data set. In the left-hand panel of Figure 2.5, the bias initially
decreases rapidly, resulting in a sharp decrease in the expected test MSE. On the other hand,
in the center panel of Figure 2.5 the true 𝑓 is close to linear, so there is only a small decrease
in bias as flexibility increases, and the test MSE only declines slightly before increasing rapidly
as the variance increases. Finally, in the right-hand panel of Figure 2.5, as flexibility increases,
there is a dramatic decline in bias because the true 𝑓 is very non-linear. There is also very little
39
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
increase in variance as flexibility increases. Consequently, the test MSE declines substantially
before experiencing a small increase as model flexibility increases.
Figure 2.4. Squared bias (blue curve), variance (orange curve), 𝑉 [𝜖](dashed line), and test
MSE (red curve) for the three data sets in Figures 2.1-2.2. The vertical dotted line indicates
the flexibility level corresponding to the smallest test MSE. Source: James et al. (2013).
The relationship between bias, variance, and test set MSE given in Equation ( 2.6) and displayed
in Figure 2.5 is referred to as the bias-variance trade-off . Good test set performance of a
statistical/machine learning method requires low variance as well as low squared bias. This is
referred to as a trade-off because it is easy to obtain a method with extremely low bias but high
variance (for instance, by drawing a curve that passes through every single training observation)
or a method with very low variance but high bias (by fitting a horizontal line to the data). The
challenge lies in finding a method for which both the variance and the squared bias are low.
In a real-life situation where 𝑓 is unobserved, it is generally impossible to explicitly compute the
test MSE, bias, or variance for a statistical/machine learning method. Nevertheless, one should
always keep the bias-variance trade-off in mind. In this course, we explore extremely flexible
methods. However, this does not guarantee that they will outperform a much simpler approach
such as linear regression.
2.2.3 The classification setting
Our discussion of model accuracy has been focused on the regression setting until now. However,
concepts we mentioned above, such as the bias-variance trade-off, transfer over to the classifica-
tion setting with only some modifications due to the fact that 𝑦𝑖 is no longer numerical.
Suppose that we seek to estimate 𝑓 based on training observations {(𝑥1, 𝑦1), (𝑥2, 𝑦2), … , (𝑥𝑛, 𝑦𝑛)},
where now 𝑦1, 𝑦2, … , 𝑦𝑛 are qualitative. The most common approach for quantifying the accuracy
of our estimate ̂𝑓 is the training error rate, the proportion of mistakes that are made if we apply
our estimate ̂𝑓 to the training observations:
1
𝑛
𝑛
∑
𝑖=1
𝐼(𝑦𝑖 ≠ ̂ 𝑦𝑖) . (2.7)
40
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
Here ̂ 𝑦𝑖 is the predicted class label for the 𝑖th observation using ̂𝑓. 𝐼(𝑦𝑖 ≠ ̂ 𝑦𝑖)is an indicator
variable that equals 1 if 𝑦𝑖 ≠ ̂ 𝑦𝑖 and zero if 𝑦𝑖 = ̂ 𝑦𝑖. If 𝐼(𝑦𝑖 ≠ ̂ 𝑦𝑖) = 0, then the 𝑖th observation was
classified correctly by our classification method; otherwise, it was misclassified. Hence, Equation
(2.7) computes the fraction of incorrect classifications.
Equation ( 2.7) is referred to as the training error rate because it is computed based on the data
that was used to train our classifier. As in the regression setting, we are most interested in the
error rates that result from applying our classifier to test observations that were not used in
training. The test error rate associated with a set of test observations of the form (𝑥0, 𝑦0)is
given by:
Ave [𝐼(𝑦0 ≠ ̂ 𝑦0)] , (2.8)
where ̂ 𝑦0 is the predicted class label that results from applying the classifier to the test observa-
tion with predictor 𝑥0. A good classifier is one for which the test error is smallest.
The Bayes classifier
It is possible to show that the test error rate given in ( 2.8) is minimized, on average, by a very
simple classifier that assigns each observation to the most likely class, given its predictor values.
In other words, we should simply assign a test observation with predictor vector 𝑥0 to the class
𝑗 for which:
𝑃 [𝑌 = 𝑗|𝑋 = 𝑥0] (2.9)
is largest. Note that ( 2.9) is a conditional probability : it is the probability that 𝑌 = 𝑗 , given
the observed predictor vector 𝑥0. This very simple classifier is called the Bayes classifier . In a
two-class problem where there are only two possible response values, say class 1 or class 2 ,
the Bayes classifier corresponds to predicting class 1 if 𝑃 [𝑌 = 1|𝑋 = 𝑥0] > 0.5, and class 2
otherwise.
Figure 2.6 provides an example using a simulated data set in a two-dimensional space consisting
of predictors 𝑋1 and 𝑋2. The orange and blue circles correspond to training observations
belonging to two classes. For each value of 𝑋1 and 𝑋2, there is a different probability of the
response being orange or blue. Since this is simulated data, we know how the data were generated,
and we can calculate the conditional probabilities for each value of 𝑋1 and 𝑋2. The orange-
shaded region reflects the set of points for which 𝑃 [𝑌 = 𝑜𝑟𝑎𝑛𝑔𝑒|𝑋]is greater than 50%, while
the blue-shaded region indicates the set of points for which the probability is below 50%. The
purple dashed line represents the points where the probability is exactly 50%. This is called the
Bayes decision boundary . The Bayes classifier’s prediction is determined by the Bayes decision
boundary; an observation that falls on the orange side of the boundary will be assigned to the
orange class, and similarly an observation on the blue side of the boundary will be assigned to
the blue class.
2.3 K-nearest neighbour (KNN)
The KNN algorithm can be applied to both classification and regression.
41
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance

orange class, and the blue background grid indicates the region where a test observation will be
assigned to the blue class. Source: James et al. (2013).

The KNN classifier first identifies the 𝐾 points in the training data that are closest to 𝑥0,
represented by 𝑁0. The classifier then estimates the conditional probability for class 𝑗 as the
fraction of points in 𝑁0 whose response values equal 𝑗:
𝑃 [𝑌 = 𝑗|𝑋 = 𝑥0] = 1
𝐾 ∑
𝑖∈𝑁0
𝐼(𝑦𝑖 = 𝑗) .
To be able to identify the closest points, we must be able to compute the distance between
the observations. Many distance function choices exist, but the Euclidean distance is typically
used. Moreover, the predictors need to be standardized otherwise predictors with the largest
magnitudes will weight more in the computation of the distance.
Finally, KNN applies Bayes’ rule and classifies the test observation 𝑥0 to the class with the
largest probability.
Figure. The KNN approach with 𝐾 = 3 for six blue observations and six orange observa-
tions.Left: A test observation at which a predicted class label is desired is shown as a black
cross. The three closest points to the test observation are identified, and it is predicted that the
test observation belongs to the most commonly-occurring class, in this case, blue. Right: The
KNN decision boundary for this example is shown in black. The blue grid indicates the region
where a test observation will be assigned to the blue class, and the orange grid indicates the
region in which it will be assigned to the orange class. Source: James et al. (2013).
For regression, KNN first identifies the 𝐾 points in the training data that are closest to 𝑥0,
represented by 𝑁0. It then estimates the value at 𝑥0 as:

where 𝑦𝑖 is the response variable corresponding to 𝑥𝑖, 𝑖 ∈ 𝑁0.
In R, KNN can be performed with the function knn() from base R or kknn() from the package
kknn. We will use the package kknn as it is more flexible (it allows us more flexibility in the
choice of the distance, as well as the option to weigh the observations).

Exercise 2.2 – Concepts
Indicate whether we generally expect the performance of a flexible statistical/machine learning
method to be better or worse than an inflexible method. Justify your answer.
• The sample size 𝑛 is extremely large, and the number of predictors 𝑝 is small.
• The number of predictors 𝑝 is extremely large, and the number of observations 𝑛 is small.
• The relationship between the predictors and response is highly non-linear.
• The variance of the error terms, i.e., 𝜎2 = 𝑉 [𝜖], is extremely high.
Solution
Q1: The sample size 𝑛 is extremely large, and the number of predictors 𝑝 is small.
A1: Since the sample size is extremely large with few predictors, there is a low risk of overfitting.
Hence, a flexible model would be more appropriate in this situation.
Q2: The number of predictors 𝑝 is extremely large, and the number of observations 𝑛 is small.
A2: A flexible model with a high number of predictors and a small number of observations is
more likely to overfit. Hence, an inflexible model is more appropriate.
Q3: The relationship between the predictors and response is highly non-linear.
A3: Non-flexible models are not usually good for predicting highly non-linear relationships.
Flexible models can adapt any non-linear function. In this case, the flexible model would be a
better choice.
Q4: The variance of the error terms, i.e., 𝜎2 = 𝑉 [𝜖], is extremely high.
A4: In this case, it could be that the model used is too flexible or not enough flexible. If 𝜎2 is
high because of a high bias, then we should try a more flexible model. If 𝜎2 is high because of
high variance of the model, then we should try an inflexible model.

Solution
f <- function(x) {
5/(x + 1) + 1e-1 * x^2 + 5
}
# Function to simulate data
simulate_data <- function(sample_size){
x <- runif(n = sample_size, min = 0, max = 10)
eps <- rnorm(n = sample_size, mean = 0, sd = sqrt(0.5))
y <- f(x) + eps
data.frame("x" = x, "y" = y)
}

# Calculate MSE
f_mse <- function(y_true, y_pred){
mean((y_true - y_pred)^2)
}
# sample test and train data from sim_data
sample_from_data <- function(data, train_size){

# Fit data and calculates the MSE
fit_and_mse <- function(sampled_data, complexity){
# fit model on train_data
fitted_mod <- lm(y ~ poly(x, degree = complexity),
data = sampled_data$train)
# predict y on train data
y_pred_train <- fitted_mod$fitted.values
# predict y on test data
y_pred_test <- predict(fitted_mod, newdata = sampled_data$test)
list("train" = f_mse(sampled_data$train$y, y_pred_train),
"test" = f_mse(sampled_data$test$y, y_pred_test))
}
## Second plot
# Make n_folds sample of train and test data and evaluate the MSE
for (sim in 1:n_folds) {
# sample test and train data from sim_data
sampled_data <- sample_from_data(simulate_data(100), train_size)
# iterate over all polynomial orders (complexity)
for (complexity in complexity_grid) {
mse_sim <- fit_and_mse(sampled_data, complexity)
mse_grid$train[complexity] <- mse_grid$train[complexity] + mse_sim$train
mse_grid$test[complexity] <- mse_grid$test[complexity] + mse_sim$test
}
}
# Calculate the mean of the MSE train and test
mse_grid$train <- mse_grid$train / n_folds
mse_grid$test <- mse_grid$test / n_folds
# plot MSE test line
plot(x = complexity_grid, y = mse_grid$test,
col = 'red',
xlab = 'Polynomial order' ,
ylab = 'Mean Squared Error' ,
type = 'l',
xlim = c(0, complexity_max),
ylim = c(min(mse_grid$train, mse_grid $test),
max(mse_grid$train, mse_grid $test)))

f_sim_data <- function(f, sample_size = 100, iseed = 2020) {
set.seed(iseed)
# Data generation process
x <- runif(n = sample_size, min = 0, max = 10)
eps <- rnorm(n = sample_size, mean = 0, sd = sqrt(0.5))
y <- f(x) + eps
data.frame(x, y)
}
f_plot_var_bias_mse <- function(f, order_vec, n_sim1, n_sim2, n_pts, max_order,
iseed){
sim_data <- f_sim_data(f, sample_size = n_pts, iseed = iseed)
# Fitting models
fit1 <- lm(y ~ poly(x, degree = order_vec[1]), data = sim_data)
fit2 <- lm(y ~ poly(x, degree = order_vec[2]), data = sim_data)
fit3 <- lm(y ~ poly(x, degree = order_vec[3]), data = sim_data)
iseed <- iseed + 1
mse_train <- array(0, dim = c(n_sim1, n_sim2, max_order))
mse_test <- array(0, dim = c(n_sim1, n_sim2, max_order))
51
2 Statistical/machine learning and model accuracy in finance
bias_test <- array(0, dim = c(n_sim1, n_sim2, max_order))
var_test <- array(0, dim = c(n_sim1, n_sim2, max_order))
sim_data_test <- f_sim_data(f, sample_size = n_sim1, iseed = iseed * iseed)
truth_test <- sim_data_test$y
for (isim1 in 1:n_sim1) {
for (isim2 in 1:n_sim2) {
# simulate new, random, training data
# this is the only random portion of the bias, var, and MSE calculations
# this allows us to calculate the expectation for a point
sim_data <- f_sim_data(f, sample_size = n_pts, iseed = iseed + isim1 + 5 * isim2)
x <- sim_data$x
truth_train <- sim_data$y

fit_model = lm(y ~ poly(x, degree = n_order), data = sim_data)
# get predictions for training data and test data
predictions_train <- predict(fit_model, newdata =
data.frame(x = x))
predictions_test <- predict(fit_model, newdata =
data.frame(x = sim_data_test$x[isim1]))
mse_train[isim1, isim2, n_order] <- mean((predictions_train - truth_train)^2)
mse_test[isim1, isim2, n_order] <- (predictions_test - truth_test[isim1])^2
bias_test[isim1, isim2, n_order] <- predictions_test - truth_test[isim1]
var_test[isim1, isim2, n_order] <- predictions_test
}
}
}
bias_mat <- apply(bias_test, c(1, 3), mean)
var_mat <- apply(var_test, c(1, 3), var)
mse_train_mat <- apply(mse_train, c(1, 3), mean)
mse_test_mat <- apply(mse_test, c(1, 3), mean)
mse_train_vec <- colMeans(mse_train_mat)
mse_test_vec <- colMeans(mse_test_mat)
bias_vec <- colMeans(bias_mat^2)
var_vec <- colMeans(var_mat)
list("sim_data" = sim_data,
"fit1" = fit1,
"fit2" = fit2,
"fit3" = fit3,
"bias_vec" = bias_vec,
"var_vec" = var_vec,
"mse_train_vec" = mse_train_vec,
"mse_test_vec" = mse_test_vec)
}

3 Linear regression
Linear regression is a very simple approach in supervised learning. It is used when the output 𝑦
is quantitative. Because of its simplicity, it often serves as an excellent introductory method to
statistical/machine learning techniques. It is, therefore, essential to have a good understanding
of linear regression before moving to more complicated models.
This chapter will discuss the linear regression model, starting with the simple linear model in
Section 3.1. We will then extend the discussion to multiple linear regression in Section 3.2.
In Section 3.3, we will consider a few questions to keep in mind when using linear regression.
Finally, we present a case study in Section 3.4.
Learning objectives
At the end of this chapter, a learner should:
– Feel comfortable explaining simple linear regression and multiple linear regression.
– Understand how to estimate the coeﬀicients of the model.
– Discuss measures of fit to determine the accuracy of the model.
– Apply linear regression to simple and more complex examples.
Resources
This chapter is based on the following references:
• An Introduction to Statistical Learning, Chapter 3 by Gareth James, Daniela Witten,
Trevor Hastie and Robert Tibshirani
• Data Mining with R: Learning with Case Studies by Luís Torgo
3.1 Simple linear regression model
Simple linear regression is an easy method that is used to predict the value of a quantitative
variable 𝑌 dependent on a simple predictor 𝑋. The relationship between the two variables is
assumed to be approximately linear. This can be expressed as:
𝑌 ≈ 𝛽 0 + 𝛽1𝑋 . (3.1)
In Equation ( 3.1), 𝛽0 and 𝛽1 are two unknown constants, the intercept and slope of the linear
model. They are known as the coeﬀicients or parameters of the model. So linear regression is a
parametric model.
54
3 Linear regression
Let us assume 𝑌 represents the weekly (computed every Wednesday) logarithmic returns for
AAPL - Apple Inc stock (obtained from adjusted close prices) from 2014-01-01 to 2019-12-31,
and 𝑋 represents the value of the returns the previous Wednesday.
A few questions we would like to answer:
• Is there a relationship between 𝑌 and 𝑋 (is the return on a given Wednesday dependent
on the return on the previous Wednesday)?
• How strong is the relationship?
We first split our data into training and test sets, as seen in Chapter 2. From the training data,
we can find the estimates ̂𝛽0 and ̂𝛽1 for the model parameters, and we can then use our model
to make predictions for the next weekly return of the stock given the current return:
̂ 𝑦 = ̂𝛽0 + ̂𝛽1𝑥 . (3.2)
Here, 𝑋 = 𝑥 and ̂ 𝑦is a prediction of 𝑌 based on 𝑥.
3.1.1 Coeﬀicients estimation
Coeﬀicients 𝛽0 and 𝛽1 need to be estimated before we can use Equation ( 3.1) to predict the next
return. Let us assume the first four years (2015-2018) are used for training, and the last year
(2019) is used for testing.
We will first build a data frame containing all the output variables as well as the predictors that
will be used throughout this chapter.
We select an illustrative set of technical indicators from those available in the package TTR
(Ulrich, 2023) - the A verage True Range (ATR), which is an indicator of the volatility of the series;
the Stochastic Momentum Index (SMI), which is a momentum indicator, the Welles Wilder’s
Directional Movement Index (ADX), the Aroon indicator that tries to identify starting trends,
the Bollinger Bands that compares the volatility over a period of time, the Chaikin Volatility, the
Close Location Value (CL V) that relates the session close to its trading range, the Arms’s Ease
of Movement Value (EMV), the MACD oscillator, the Money Flow Index (MFI), the Parabolic
Stop-and-Reverse, and the Volatility indicator. More details and references on these and other
indicators can be found in the respective help pages of the functions implementing them in the
package TTR.
Most of these indicators produce several values that together are used for making trading de-
cisions. As mentioned before, we do not plan to use these indicators for trading. As such, we
have carried out some post-processing of the output of the TTR functions to obtain a single
indicator score for each one.
We select as well lagged values ( 0:3) of the returns as predictors (here lag 0 represents the
current return).
# Load libraries and define a few technical indicators
library("TTR")
# A few technical indicators functions from TTR
f_ATR <- function(x)
55

EMA(CLV(HLC(x)))[, 1]

EMV(cbind(Hi(x), Lo(x)), Vo(x))[, 2]
f_MACD <- function(x)
MACD(Cl(x))[, 2]
f_MFI <- function(x)
MFI(HLC(x), Vo(x))
f_SAR <- function(x)
SAR(cbind(Hi(x), Cl(x))) [, 1]
f_SMI <- function(x)
SMI(HLC(x))[, "SMI"]
f_Volat <- function(x)
volatility(OHLC(x), calc = "garman")[, 1]

We will now discuss the significance of the coeﬀicients.
3.1.3 Accuracy of the coeﬀicient estimates
We use a hypothesis test to check whether the coeﬀicient is statistically significant. That is, we
would like to check the strength of the relationship between predictor and response variable. In
the single coeﬀicient case:
𝐻0 ∶ ̂𝛽1 = 0 ( Null hypothesis )
𝐻1 ∶ ̂𝛽1 ≠ 0 ( Alternative hypothesis ) (3.5)
To test the truthfulness of 𝐻0, we compute the t-stat:
𝑡 =
̂𝛽1
𝑆𝐸( ̂𝛽1)
,
where 𝑆𝐸( ̂𝛽𝑖)is the standard error associated with ̂𝛽𝑖.
The t-stat measures the number of standard deviations that ̂𝛽𝑖 is away from 0. If 𝑡 is large
(equivalently, the p-value is small), we can reject the null hypothesis and be sure that there is a
relationship.
summary(model_slr)$coefficients
## Estimate Std. Error t value Pr(>|t|)
## (Intercept) 0.001965504 0.002115665 0.9290244 0.3539744
## aapl_adjclose_lag0 0.111390037 0.069452009 1.6038418 0.1102960
Looking at the p-value, we conclude that, at the 1% significance, we cannot reject 𝛽1 = 0 and
𝛽0 = 0.
Inference about the parameters is performed under the assumption that the residuals are
independent and identically distributed (i.i.d.), following a Gaussian distribution centered
at zero and a constant standard deviation. In finance, the hypothesis of homoscedasticity
is often violated, and the Newey-West or HAC approach needs to be considered. When
time series are involved, the hypothesis of independence is often violated. An analysis
of the time series property of the residual is suggested. These considerations matter for
inference purposes (the t-tests); for prediction purposes, it is less of an issue.

squares? How would you tackle data with outliers? Try to use the function rlm() from
the MASS package. Have also a look at the set of functions available in the robustbase

3.1.4 Population regression line vs. least squares line
The model given by 𝑌 = 𝛽 0 + 𝛽1𝑋1 + 𝜖 defines the population regression line, which is the best
linear approximation to the true relationship between 𝑋 and 𝑌 .
The least squares regression coeﬀicient estimates ̂𝑌 = ̂𝛽0 + ̂𝛽1𝑋1 characterize the least squares
line.
In real-world problems, we would like to know the real coeﬀicients 𝛽0, 𝛽1, that is the population
line. The true relationship is generally not known for real data, but the least squares line can
always be computed using the coeﬀicient estimates ̂𝛽0, ̂𝛽1. Hence, our estimated coeﬀicients can
be considered as the best guesses for 𝛽0, 𝛽1 and the resulting ̂𝑌 as the best guess for 𝑌 .
3.1.5 Assessing the accuracy of the model
Assuming we have rejected the null hypothesis in favor of the alternative hypothesis in ( 3.5),
we might want quantify the extent to which the model fits the data. The quality of a linear
regression fit is typically assessed using two related quantities: The residual standard error
(RSE) and the 𝑅2 statistic.
A measure of fit: RSE
The residual standard error (RSE) is an estimate of the standard deviation of 𝜖. Roughly
speaking, it is the average amount that the response will deviate from the true regression line:
RSE = √ 1
𝑛 − 2RSS = √ 1
𝑛 − 2
𝑛
∑
𝑖=1
(𝑦𝑖 − ̂ 𝑦𝑖)2 ,
where RSS is the residual sum of squares, and 𝑛 is the number of observations.
The RSE is considered a measure of the lack of fit of the model to the data. If the predictions
obtained using the model are very close to the true outcome values, then RSE will be small, and
we can conclude that the model fits the data very well.
rse <- sqrt(sum(residuals(model_slr) ^ 2) / df.residual(model_slr))
print(rse)
## [1] 0.03029638
64
3 Linear regression
A measure of fit: 𝑅2
The RSE provides an absolute measure of the lack of fit of the model to the data. However,
since it is measured in the units of 𝑌 , it is not always clear what constitutes a good RSE.
The 𝑅2 statistic provides an (indirect) alternative measure of fit. It takes the form of a propor-
tion, the proportion of variability in 𝑌 that can be explained using 𝑋. So it always takes on a
value between 0 and 1, and is independent of the scale of 𝑌 . It is written as:
𝑅2 = TSS − RSS
TSS = 1 − RSS
TSS ,
where TSS = ∑(𝑦𝑖 − ̄ 𝑦)2 is the total sum of squares and RSS is the residual sum of squares.
𝑅2 = 0 indicates that no variability in 𝑌 is explained by the model, while 𝑅2 = 1 is the opposite
(perfect fit).
summary(model_slr)$r.squared
## [1] 0.01245234
From the value of 𝑅2, we conclude that there is almost no variability in the returns is explained
by the returns in the previous week.
RSE and 𝑅2 are statistical measures that give us an idea of how well our model performs.
However, for financial applications, the returns obtained from applying our models could
ultimately be the metric by which a model is judged. Backtesting might give an idea of
the profits that can be obtained from using our models.
3.2 Multiple linear regression model
The multiple linear regression model is given by:
𝑌 = 𝛽 0 + 𝛽1𝑋1 + 𝛽2𝑋2 + … + 𝛽𝑝𝑋𝑝 + 𝜖 ,
where 𝑋𝑗 is the 𝑗th predictor (𝑗 = 1, … , 𝑝), 𝑌 is the response variable, 𝜖 is the error term.
Coeﬀicient 𝛽0 is the intercept (i.e., the average value for 𝑌 if all the 𝑋s are zero). Coeﬀicient
𝛽𝑗 is the slope for the 𝑗th variable 𝑋𝑗. 𝛽𝑗 quantifies the association between that variable and
the response. It is the average increase in 𝑌 when 𝑋𝑗 is increased by one unit and all other 𝑋s
are held constant.
The parameters 𝛽0, 𝛽1, … , 𝛽𝑝 are unknown and must be estimated. We do so by using the least
squares approach. That is, we aim to find the parameters that minimize the sum of squared
residuals (RSS):
RSS =
𝑛
∑
𝑖=1
(𝑦𝑖 − ̂ 𝑦𝑖)2 =
𝑛
∑
𝑖=1
(𝑦𝑖 − ̂𝛽0 − ̂𝛽1𝑥𝑖1 − … − ̂𝛽𝑝𝑥𝑖𝑝)2 .
The hypothesis test for multiple linear regression is:
65
3 Linear regression
𝐻0 ∶𝛽1 = 𝛽2 = … = 𝛽 𝑝 = 0
𝐻1 ∶at least one 𝛽𝑖 is non-zero
This hypothesis test is performed by computing the F-statistic, written as:
𝐹 = (TSS − RSS)/𝑝
RSS/(𝑛 − 𝑝 − 1).
If the F-statistic is close to one, it means that there is no relationship between the response and
predictors. Therefore, the larger the better.
It is often assumed that 𝐹 has a Fisher distribution, which is true is the residuals are i.i.d
Gaussian centered at zero.
3.2.1 Population line vs. least squares line
In analogy to the simple linear regression, we define the population regression line as:
𝑌 = 𝛽 0 + 𝛽1𝑋1 + 𝛽2𝑋2 + … + 𝛽𝑝𝑋𝑝 + 𝜖 .
The least squares line is:
̂𝑌 = ̂𝛽0 + ̂𝛽1𝑋1 + ̂𝛽2𝑋2 + … + ̂𝛽𝑝𝑋𝑝 .
We would like to know the real coeﬀicients 𝛽0, … 𝛽𝑝, that is, the population line. Instead, what
we can compute are ̂𝛽0, … ̂𝛽𝑝, the coeﬀicients of the least squares line. Hence, our estimated
coeﬀicients can be considered as the best guesses for 𝛽0, … , 𝛽𝑝, and the resulting ̂𝑌 as the best
guess for 𝑌 .
We will now apply a multiple linear regression to the AAPL returns. We first have a look once

We would like to forecast the returns using all the columns except the date. The formula to carry
this out is in the snippet below. The expression ~ . tells lm() to regress aapl_adjclose_lead
on all the columns in the data frame, -date tells lm() to exclude the date column.
# Build the formula to use for the multiple linear regression
# Formula: output variable = aapl_adjclose_lead
# Predictor : ~ . means all the predictors in the data.frame
# : - date means except date column
fmla_mlr <- aapl_adjclose_lead ~ . -date
# Fit the model
model_mlr <- lm(formula = fmla_mlr, data = df_aapl_train)
# Get the R-square, adjusted R-square, and the coefficients of the model
