3.3 Important questions
Do all the predictors help explain 𝑌 , or is only a subset of the predictors useful?
This is known as the variable selection problem . To answer this question, consider all the 2𝑝
possible models that can be built with the available variables (i.e., if 𝑝 = 2 , you can consider
four models: one without any predictor, one including only 𝑋1, the other with 𝑋2 alone, and
finally one with both of them). Then, compare them using criteria such as BIC, AIC, Mallow’s
𝐶𝑝, and adjusted 𝑅2. This approach can be tricky for large 𝑝. This will be discussed later.
How well does the model fit the data?
67
3 Linear regression
We can use the criteria just introduced, such as 𝑅2 and RSE. However, bear in mind that 𝑅2
will always increase when more variables are added to the model, even if those variables are only
weakly associated with the response.
For prediction purposes, the 𝑅2 should be computed on a test set or out-of-sample data.
Is least squares regression suitable for a large number of predictors 𝑝?
For large values of 𝑝 (in the extreme case 𝑛 << 𝑝, 𝑛 is the number of observations), the matrix
in the least-square method might not be invertible. Other methods to find the coeﬀicients ̂𝛽𝑖
will be more suitable in this case, and we will study a few of them later.
3.4 Case study: Stock picking based on regression
From the top 20 constituents of the NASDAQ 100, we will build a long/short portfolio. Linear
regression will be used to forecast the weekly returns of the stocks (on Wednesdays).
top_nasdaq <- c("AAPL", "MSFT", "AMZN", "META", "GOOGL",
"GOOG", "TSLA", "NVDA", "NFLX", "ADBE",
"INTC", "CSCO", "CMCSA", "PEP", "AMGN",
"COST", "TMUS", "AVGO", "QCOM", "CHTR")
# The file utils.R contains a function that will help us download the data
library("here")
source(file = here("code", "utils.R"))
from <- "2014-01-01"
to <- "2019-12-31"
# Fetch the stocks with tickers in top_nasdaq
stock_ind <- lapply(top_nasdaq, f_fetch_ind, from = from, to = to)
names(stock_ind) <- top_nasdaq
n_stocks <- length(top_nasdaq)
# Initialize an empty list of length n_stocks
model_list <- vector("list", n_stocks)
names(model_list) <- top_nasdaq
# Define training data and test data dates
idx_train <- stock_ind$AAPL$date >= "2015-01-01" & stock_ind$AAPL$date < "2019-01-01"
idx_test <- stock_ind$AAPL$date >= "2019-01-01" & stock_ind$AAPL$date < "2019-12-01"
After downloading the data and defining the train and test indices, we build a multiple linear
regression model for each stock and train them on the training set.
# Fitting a multiple linear model for each stock
for (i in 1:n_stocks) {
# Base name of a few columns in the training set data frame
# E.g: Upper case characters will be transformed into lower case characters,
68
3 Linear regression
# and we get rid of any character not a letter nor a digit.
base_name <- tolower(gsub("[[:punct:]]", "", top_nasdaq[i]))
# Multiple linear regression formula
fmla_mlr <- paste0(paste0(base_name, "_adjclose_lead"), " ~ . " ,
"- date - direction_lead" )
fmla_mlr <- as.formula(fmla_mlr)
# Pick the training set for stock i
df_train <- stock_ind[[top_nasdaq[i]]][idx_train, ]
# Fit a multiple linear regression for stock i
model_mlr <- lm(formula = fmla_mlr, data = df_train)
# Save the model for stock i to the list model_list
model_list[[top_nasdaq[i]]] <- model_mlr
}
We then build two key objects: pred_mat, the matrix containing the returns predictions over the
testing window, and rets_mat_test the matrix containing the realized returns over the testing
window.
# Prediction matrix (for the predicted returns from the model)

nrow = sum(idx_test),
ncol = n_stocks)
# Returns matrix (for the realized returns)

We will build several benchmark strategies to compare with the regression-based strategy. Short
positions are allowed. We consider the following approaches:
• The equally weighted portfolio: Each stock is attributed a weight 1/20 at the start and is
not rebalanced.
• The mean-variance portfolio, which is the set of weights 𝑤 that give the best expected
return-variance trade-off for a given risk-preference parameter 𝛾 > 0 :
𝑤 = 1
𝛾 Σ−1𝜇 .
Here 𝜇 is the vector of expected returns, and Σ is the covariance matrix.
• The maximum diversification portfolio, which is the set of weights 𝑤 that maximize the
diversification ratio:
𝐷𝑅(𝑤) = 𝑤′𝜎√
𝑤′Σ𝑤
≥ 1 ,
where 𝜎 is the vector of individual stock’s volatility.
# Define dimensions for the returns matrix for the training set.
df_train <- stock_ind[[top_nasdaq[1]]][idx_train,]

nrow = nrow(df_train),
ncol = n_stocks)

rets_mat_train[, i] <- df_train[[paste0(base_name,"_adjclose_lead")]]
}
# Mean and covariance matrix of the stock returns (training set)
mu <- colMeans(rets_mat_train)
Sigma <- cov(rets_mat_train)
# Equally weighted strategy
w_ew <- rep(1 / n_stocks, n_stocks)
port_rets_ew <- Return.portfolio(R = rets_mat_test, weights = w_ew)

We compare these strategies with our stock-picking regression-based approach. We select
n_picks stocks and construct a weekly rebalanced portfolio where we invest an equal-weight
2/n_picks in the n_picks stocks predicted to have the highest returns and short an equal
weight 1/n_picks in the n_picks stocks predicted to have the lowest returns for the upcoming
week.
library("doBy")
# Pick 3 stocks to short, 3 stocks to buy.
n_picks <- 3
# Initialize returns matrix and return ticker for the picks
pick_rets_mat <- pick_ticker <- matrix(data = NA,
ncol = 2 * n_picks,
nrow = nrow(pred_mat))
# Going long the n_picks best performing stock from our prediction and
# shorting the n_picks worst performing stock
for (i in 1:nrow(rets_mat_test)) {
# which.maxn (which.minn) from package doBy give 3 largest (smallest) value of
# a vector. Long picks in position 1:n_picks
# short picks in (n_picks +1 ):(2 * n_picks)
pick_ticker[i, 1:n_picks] <-
top_nasdaq[which.maxn(coredata(pred_mat[i, ]), n = n_picks)]
pick_ticker[i, (n_picks + 1):(2 * n_picks)] <-
top_nasdaq[which.minn(coredata(pred_mat[i, ]), n = n_picks)]
# Associate the corresponding returns to the tickers chosen (picked)
pick_rets_mat[i, 1:n_picks] <-
rets_mat_test[i, pick_ticker[i, 1:n_picks]]

rets_mat_test[i, pick_ticker[i, (n_picks + 1):(2 * n_picks)]]
}
# Set a name to the column of the matrix pick_rets_mat
colnames(pick_rets_mat) <- paste0("rets", 1:(2 * n_picks))
# Transform to an xts object
pick_rets_mat <- xts(x = pick_rets_mat, order.by = index(rets_mat_test))
# Use Return.portfolio() to compute the returns of a portfolio
# with weights 2/n_picks (long) in the predicted best performing stocks
# and weights -1/n_picks (short) in the predicted worst performing stocks.
w_pick <- c(rep(2 / n_picks, n_picks), rep(-1 / n_picks, n_picks))
port_rets_pick <- Return.portfolio(R = pick_rets_mat, weights = w_pick)

Solution of Your Turn 3.3
X <- df_aapl_train$aapl_adjclose_lag0
Y <- df_aapl_train$aapl_adjclose_lead
RSS <- function(beta_0, beta_1){
sum((Y - (beta_0 + beta_1 * X))^2)
}
vbeta_0 <- seq(-0.1, 0.1, by = 0.001)
vbeta_1 <- seq(0, 0.2, by = 0.001)
plot(vbeta_0, sapply(vbeta_0, RSS, beta_1 = c(b_1)), type = 'l',
xlab = 'beta_0', ylab = 'RSS')
points(x = b_0, y = RSS(c(b_0), c(b_1)), col = "red")
plot(vbeta_1, sapply(vbeta_1, RSS, beta_0 = c(b_0)), type = 'l',
xlab = 'beta_1', ylab = 'RSS')
points(x = b_1, y = RSS(c(b_0), c(b_1)), col = "red")

is the number of observations, and 𝑝 is the number of parameters (including the intercept) to
estimate. An estimated parameter will then follow a distribution 𝑆𝑡𝑢𝑑𝑒𝑛𝑡(𝑑𝑓 = 𝑁 − 𝑝).

Exercise 3.2 – Theory 2
Using Equation ( 3.4), show that in the case of simple linear regression, the least squares line
always passes through the point ( ̄ 𝑥, ̄ 𝑦).
77
3 Linear regression
Solution
From Equation ( 3.4), we have:
̂𝛽0 = ̄ 𝑦 − ̂𝛽1 ̄ 𝑥 ⇔ ̄ 𝑦 =̂𝛽0 + ̂𝛽1 ̄ 𝑥 .
So ( ̄ 𝑥, ̄ 𝑦)satisfies the equation of the least squares line and so the latter passes through the point
( ̄ 𝑥, ̄ 𝑦).

# ADX

3 Linear regression

idx_test <- "2019/"

4 Classification
In this chapter, we discuss classification. Classification problems occur often, perhaps even more
so than regression problems. Some examples:
1. An online banking service must be able to determine whether or not a transaction being
performed on the site is fraudulent on the basis of the user’s IP address, past transaction
history, etc.
2. A bank/credit card company may want to determine whether or not a customer is likely
to default on his credit card debt using the number of credit cards, the amount due, the
marital status, the employment history and more.
3. A credit rating agency would like to classify companies’ bonds in different credit classes
(AAA, AA, A, BBB, BB, B, etc.).
In Section 4.1, we discuss the limitation of linear regression when the response is qualitative.
Section 4.2 introduces logistic regression as an alternative to linear regression for a qualitative
response. We survey multiple metrics to assess the performance of our models in Section 4.3.
We conclude the chapter with a case study in Section 4.4.
Learning objectives
At the end of this chapter, a learner should:
– Feel comfortable explaining classification problems.
– Understand logistic regression.
– Discuss measures of fit to determine the accuracy of the model.
– Apply classification to simple and more complex examples.
Resources
This chapter is based on the following reference:
• An Introduction to Statistical Learning, Chapter 4 by Gareth James, Daniela Witten,
Trevor Hastie and Robert Tibshirani
4.1 Why not linear regression?
Linear regression is not appropriate in the case of a qualitative response. Why not?
Suppose that we are trying to predict whether to Buy, Hold, or Sell the stock of a company
based on the set of predictors seen in Chapter 3. Writing these three actions as a quantitative
response variable, 𝑌 , we get:
83
4 Classification
𝑌 =
⎧{
⎨{⎩
1 if Sell
2 if Hold
3 if Buy .
We could use least squares to fit a linear regression model to the reformulated problem, which
is to predict 𝑌 based on a set of predictors 𝑋1, … , 𝑋𝑝. Unfortunately, this coding implies an
ordering on the outcomes, putting Hold in between Sell and Buy and implying that the difference
between Hold and Sell is the same as the difference between Buy and Hold. In practice, there
is no particular reason that this needs to be the case. For instance, one could choose:
𝑌 =
⎧{
⎨{⎩
1 if Buy
2 if Sell
3 if Hold ,
which would imply a totally different relationship among the three conditions. Each of these
codings would produce fundamentally different linear models that would ultimately lead to
different sets of predictions on test observations.
The situation is better for a binary (two levels) qualitative response. For a binary instance,
perhaps there are only two possibilities ( Buy or Sell). We could then potentially use the
dummy variable approach to code the response as follows:
𝑌 = { 0 if Sell
1 if Buy .
We could then fit a linear regression to this binary response, buy the stock if ̂𝑌 > 0.5 , and sell
it otherwise. In the binary case, it is easy to show that even if we flip the above coding, linear
regression will produce the same final predictions.
Y our T urn 4.1. Show that flipping the coding does produce the same predictions.
4.2 Logistic regression
Logistic Regression, StatQuest
Consider the S&P 500 index, where the direction of the index is either Up or Down. Rather than
modeling this direction 𝑌 directly, logistic regression models the probability that 𝑌 belongs to
a particular category.
For the S&P 500 data, logistic regression models the probability of the stock going up. For
example, the probability of the direction of the returns to be Up given the volatility ( Volat) can
be written as:
𝑃 [𝐷𝑖𝑟𝑒𝑐𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 = 𝑈 𝑝|𝑉 𝑜𝑙𝑎𝑡] .
84
4 Classification
The values of 𝑃 [𝐷𝑖𝑟𝑒𝑐𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 = 𝑈 𝑝|𝑉 𝑜𝑙𝑎𝑡], which we abbreviate 𝑝(𝑉 𝑜𝑙𝑎𝑡), will range between 0 and
1. Then for any given value of Volat, a prediction can be made for the 𝐷𝑖𝑟𝑒𝑐𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛. For example,
one might predict 𝐷𝑖𝑟𝑒𝑐𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 = 𝑈 𝑝for any returns for which 𝑝(𝑉 𝑜𝑙𝑎𝑡) > 0.5. Alternatively, if
we would like to be conservative in predicting an Up direction for the stock, we can increase the
threshold such as 𝑝(𝑉 𝑜𝑙𝑎𝑡) > 0.8.
4.2.1 The logistic model
How should we model the relationship between 𝑝(𝑋) = 𝑃 [𝑌 = 1|𝑋]and 𝑋? (For convenience,
we use the generic 0/1 coding for the response). We can use a linear regression model to represent
these probabilities:
𝑝(𝑋) = 𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋 . (4.1)
If we use this approach to predict Direction = Up using volatility, then we obtain the model
shown in Figure 4.1. The problem with this approach is that by extending the regression line,
we could possibly end up with predicted probabilities greater than 1 or less than 0 (right-hand
side of Figure 4.1).
library("here")
source(file = here("code", "utils.R"))

to <- "2019-12-31"

Probability of positive returns
-4 -2 0 2 4
-1 0 1 2
Volatility
Probability of positive returns
Figure 4.1. On the left: linear regression applied to modelling probabilities. On the right:
extending the limit of the volatility, the probabilities can take values less than 0 or greater than
1.
These predictions are unrealistic since the true probability of the stock going up, regardless of
volatility, must fall between 0 and 1. Any time a straight line is fit to a binary response that is
coded as 0 or 1, in principle, we can always predict 𝑝(𝑋) < 0for some values of 𝑋 and 𝑝(𝑋) > 1
for others (unless the range of 𝑋 is limited).
To avoid this problem, we must model 𝑝(𝑋)using a function that gives outputs between 0 and
1 for all values of 𝑋. Many functions meet this description. In logistic regression, we use the
logistic function :
𝑝(𝑋) = exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
1 + exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋). (4.2)
To fit the model ( 4.2), we use the maximum likelihood method. The right-hand panel of Figure
4.2 illustrates the fit of the logistic regression model to the S&P500 data. Notice that for high
volatility, we now predict the probability as close to, but never above 1. Likewise, for low
(negative) volatility, we predict a Direction = Up probability, close to but never below 0. The
logistic function will always produce an S-shaped curve of this form so that we will obtain a
sensible prediction regardless of the value of 𝑋. We also see that the logistic model can better
capture the range of probabilities than the linear regression model in the left-hand plot.
86
4 Classification

We can show that:
𝑝(𝑋)
1 − 𝑝(𝑋)= exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋) . (4.3)
87
4 Classification
By taking the logarithm on both sides of Equation ( 4.3) we get:
log ( 𝑝(𝑋)
1 − 𝑝(𝑋)) = 𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋 . (4.4)
The left-hand side is called the log-odds or logit. We see that the logistic regression model ( 4.2)
has a logit that is linear in 𝑋.
Y our T urn 4.2. Show ( 4.3).
In a linear regression model, 𝛽1 gives the average change in 𝑌 associated with a one-unit increase
in 𝑋. In contrast, in a logistic regression model, increasing 𝑋 by one unit changes the log-odds
by 𝛽1 in Equation ( 4.4), or equivalently it multiplies the odds by exp (𝛽1)in Equation ( 4.3).
However, because the relationship between 𝑝(𝑋)and 𝑋 in Equation ( 4.2) is not a straight line,
𝛽1 does not correspond to the change in 𝑝(𝑋)associated with a one-unit increase in 𝑋. The
amount that 𝑝(𝑋)changes due to a one-unit change in 𝑋 will depend on the current value of
𝑋. But regardless of the value of 𝑋, if 𝛽1 is positive, then increasing 𝑋 will be associated with
increasing 𝑝(𝑋), and if 𝛽1 is negative, then increasing 𝑋 will be associated with decreasing 𝑝(𝑋).
The fact that there is not a straight-line relationship between 𝑝(𝑋)and 𝑋, and the fact that
the rate of change in 𝑝(𝑋)per unit change in 𝑋 depends on the current value of 𝑋, can also be
seen by inspection of the right-hand panel of Figure 4.2
4.2.2 Estimating the regression coeﬀicients
The coeﬀicients 𝛽0 and 𝛽1 in Equation ( 4.2) are unknown and must be estimated based on
the available training data. In Chapter 3, we used the least squares approach to estimate the
unknown linear regression coeﬀicients. Although we could use (non-linear) least squares to fit
the model ( 4.4), the more general method of maximum likelihood is preferred since it has better
statistical properties.
Y our T urn 4.3. List the statistical properties of maximum likelihood estimators.
The basic intuition behind using maximum likelihood to fit a logistic regression model is as
follows: We try to find ̂𝛽0 and ̂𝛽1 such that plugging these estimates into the model for 𝑝(𝑋),
given in Equation ( 4.2), yields a number close to one for all the weeks with positive returns,
and a number close to zero for all the weeks with non-positive returns. This intuition can be
formalized using a mathematical expression called a likelihood function:
𝑙(𝛽0, 𝛽1) = ∏
𝑖∶𝑦𝑖 =1
𝑝(𝑥𝑖) ∏
𝑖′∶𝑦𝑖 ′ =0
(1 − 𝑝(𝑥𝑖′)) . (4.5)
The estimates ̂𝛽0 and ̂𝛽1 are chosen to maximize this likelihood function.
Maximum likelihood is a very general approach used to fit many of the non-linear models we
examine throughout this book. In the linear regression setting, the least squares approach is a
special case of maximum likelihood.
Below, we show the coeﬀicient estimates and related information that result from fitting a
logistic regression model on the S&P 500 data to predict the probability of Direction = Up
using Volat.

Once the coeﬀicients have been estimated, it is easy to compute the probability for any given
Volat. For example, using the coeﬀicient estimates obtained above, we predict that the proba-
bility of an Up stock movement in one week with Volatility = 0.35 is:
̂ 𝑝(0.35) =exp( ̂𝛽0 + ̂𝛽1 × 0.35)
1 + exp( ̂𝛽0 + ̂𝛽1 × 0.35)
= 0.69 . (4.6)

We can use qualitative predictors with the logistic regression model using the dummy variable
approach seen previously. For example, we will try to predict the direction of the S&P 500
returns a week from now based on the current direction of the S&P 500 returns ( Up or Down).
To fit the model, we could simply create a dummy variable that takes on a value of 1 if the
current weekly return is Up and 0 if it is Down. R handles the conversion using as.factor().
The logistic regression model that results from predicting the direction of the next week return
from the current return can be seen below.

4.2.4 Multiple logistic regression
We now tackle the problem of predicting a binary response using multiple predictors. By analogy
with the extension from simple to multiple linear regression, we can generalize Equation ( 4.4)
as follows:
log ( 𝑝(𝑋)
1 − 𝑝(𝑋)) = 𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋1 + … + 𝛽𝑝𝑋𝑝 , (4.7)
where 𝑋 = (𝑋1, … , 𝑋𝑝)′ are 𝑝 predictors. Equation ( 4.7) can be rewritten as:
𝑝(𝑋) = exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋1 + … + 𝛽𝑝𝑋𝑝)
1 + exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋1 + … + 𝛽𝑝𝑋𝑝). (4.8)
Again, we use the maximum likelihood method to estimate 𝛽0, 𝛽1, … , 𝛽𝑝.

4 Classification
4.2.5 Logistic regression for multiclasses
Quite often, we would like to classify a response variable with more than two classes. For
example, we may want to either Buy, Hold, or Sell S&P 500 stocks depending on whether the
return for the next week is bigger (in absolute value) than a certain threshold. Here, we are
trying to model both 𝑃 [𝑌 =Buy|𝑋]and 𝑃 [𝑌 =Sell|𝑋], with the remaining 𝑃 [𝑌 =Hold|𝑋] =
1 − 𝑃 [𝑌 = Buy|𝑋] − 𝑃 [𝑌 = Sell|𝑋]. The two-class logistic regression models discussed in
the previous sections have multiple-class extensions, but in practice, they tend not to be used.
Alternatives such as linear discriminant analysis are preferred.
4.3 Assessing the accuracy of the model
Various classification metrics can be used to assess the accuracy of a classification task. We
discuss a few of those below.
Let actual be the actual (true) label of the observation (taking value 0 or 1), and predicted be
the predicted label. We define the following:
• True Positive (TP): the number of observations with true label 1 and predicted label 1.
• False Positive (FP): the number of observations with true label 0 and predicted label 1.
• False Negative (FN): the number of observations with true label 1 and predicted label 0.
• True Negative (TN): the number of observations with true label 0 and predicted label 0.
4.3.1 Confusion matrix
The confusion matrix is:
Confusion matrix = (𝑇 𝑁 𝐹 𝑁
𝐹 𝑃 𝑇 𝑃 ) .
4.3.2 Accuracy
Accuracy = 𝑇 𝑃 + 𝑇 𝑁
𝑇 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑁 + 𝑇 𝑁 .
The accuracy is the fraction of observations correctly identified by our classifier. Typically, the
higher the accuracy, the better the classifier. However, accuracy can be misleading when dealing
with an imbalanced dataset (where one class occurs more often than the other).
4.3.3 Classification error rate
Classification error rate = 𝐹 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑁
𝑇 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑁 + 𝑇 𝑁 .
The classification error rate is the fraction of observations incorrectly identified by the classifier.
The lower the value, the better, but similar to accuracy, it can be misleading when dealing with
imbalanced datasets.
91
4 Classification
4.3.4 Precision
Precision = 𝑇 𝑃
𝑇 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑃 .
Assuming there is a class that we care about more than the other (we will refer to it as the
positive class), precision is the ratio of the observations that belong to the positive class that
were correctly identified to the total number of observations predicted as positive by our classifier.
We have 0 ≤ Precision ≤ 1 and good classifiers will usually have high precision.
4.3.5 Sensitivity
Sensitivity = Recall = 𝑇 𝑃
𝑇 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑁 .
Sensitivity is the fraction of the positive class correctly identified by our classifier. We have
0 ≤ Sensitivity ≤ 1, and good classifiers will usually have high sensitivity.
4.3.6 Specificity
Specifity = 𝑇 𝑁
𝑇 𝑁 + 𝐹 𝑃 .
It is the proportion of the negative class that is correctly identified. We have 0 ≤ Specificity ≤
1.
4.3.7 𝐹1 score
𝐹1 = 2 × Precision × Sensitivity
Precision + Sensitivity .
𝐹1 score is the weighted average of the Precision and Sensitivity. It takes both false positives
and false negatives into account. The drawback of 𝐹1 is that it does not take into account true
negatives. We have 0 ≤ 𝐹 1 ≤ 1.
4.3.8 Cohen’s 𝜅
𝜅 = 𝑝𝑜 − 𝑝𝑒
1 − 𝑝𝑒
𝑝𝑜 = 𝑇 𝑃 + 𝑇 𝑁
𝑇 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑁 + 𝑇 𝑁
𝑝𝑒 = (𝑇 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑁 )(𝑇 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑃 ) + (𝐹 𝑃 + 𝑇 𝑁 )(𝐹 𝑁 + 𝑇 𝑁 )
(𝑇 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑃 + 𝐹 𝑁 + 𝑇 𝑁 )2
where 𝑝𝑜 is the observed level of agreement, and 𝑝𝑒 is the expected level of agreement. A perfect
classifier will have 𝜅 = 1 , whereas a random classifier (picking the class randomly) will have
𝜅 = 0. It is possible to have 𝜅 < 0.
All these functions are available in the package caret (Kuhn, 2024).

4.3.9 ROC curve
ROC and AUC, Clearly Explained!, StatQuest
A ROC curve (receiver operating characteristic curve) is a graph showing the performance
of a classification model at all classification thresholds. It is the plot of Sensitivity versus
Specificity (both defined earlier). AUC stands for “Area under the ROC Curve” . AUC
measures the area underneath the ROC curve. AUC ranges in value from 0 to 1. A perfect
classifier has an AUC of 1, whereas a classifier that is always wrong has an AUC of 0. The curve
can be computed and displayed using the package pROC (Robin et al. , 2023).
library("pROC")
# ?pROC::roc()
# This function assumes that the second class is the event of interest,
# so we reverse the labels.
rocCurve <- roc(
response = df_sp500[idx_test, "direction_lead"],
predictor = mlogr_pred$prob,
levels = levels(df_sp500[idx_test, "direction_lead"])
)
# ?pROC::auc()
auc(rocCurve)
## Area under the curve: 0.543
par(mfrow = c(1, 1))
plot(rocCurve, legacy.axes = TRUE)

library("doBy")

# Loading the data with the stock data
load(file = here("data", "nasdaq20.rda"))
n_stocks <- length(top_nasdaq)
# Create an empty list to store the logistic regression model for each stock
model_list <- vector("list", n_stocks)
names(model_list) <- top_nasdaq
# Define training and test sets date (index)
idx_train <- stock_ind$AAPL$date >= "2015-01-01" & stock_ind$AAPL$date < "2019-01-01"
idx_test <- stock_ind$AAPL$date >= "2019-01-01" & stock_ind$AAPL$date < "2019-12-01"
# Fitting a multiple logistic regression model for each stock
for (i in 1:n_stocks) {
base_name <- tolower(gsub("[[:punct:]]", "", top_nasdaq[i]))
# Set the training set
df_train <- stock_ind[[top_nasdaq[i]]][idx_train, ]
# Set the formula for the multiple logistic regression
fmla_mlogr <- paste0("direction_lead", " ~. " ,
"- date - " , paste0(base_name,"_adjclose_lead"))
fmla_mlogr <- as.formula(fmla_mlogr)
# Fitting
model_mlogr <- glm(formula = fmla_mlogr,
data = df_train,
family = binomial(link = "logit"))
# Store the fitted model in the list
model_list[[top_nasdaq[i]]] <- model_mlogr
}
# Matrices initialization
df_test <- stock_ind[[top_nasdaq[1]]][idx_test, ]
# Predicted probability of an up-movement matrix
pred_prob_mat <- matrix(NA, nrow = nrow(df_test) , ncol = n_stocks)

4 Classification
# Realized returns matrix

for (i in 1:n_stocks) {
# Set test train
df_test <- stock_ind[[top_nasdaq[i]]][idx_test, ]
# Predict the probability of an up-movement

type = "response")

}

ncol = 2 * n_picks,

# picking n_picks (short) lowest upward probability stock from our predictions

# Ticker of the n_picks highest upward probabilities

top_nasdaq[which.minn(coredata(pred_prob_mat[i, ]), n = n_picks)]
# Save realized returns corresponding to the tickers selected above

}

# Compute returns of a portfolio with weights 2/n_picks (long)
# in the highest upward probabilities stocks and

Solution of Your Turn 4.1
Let’s define
𝑌1 = {0 if Sell
1 if Buy .
and
𝑌2 = {0 if Buy
1 if Sell .
We can rewrite 𝑌2 = 1 − 𝑌1. Hence, we buy if ̂𝑌2 < 0.5. This is equivalent to saying
̂𝑌2 < 0.5
− ̂𝑌2 > −0.5
1 − ̂𝑌2 > 1 − 0.5
̂𝑌1 > 0.5

Solution of Your Turn 4.2
From Equation ( 4.2), we can find Equation ( 4.3).
𝑝(𝑋) = exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
1 + exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
𝑝(𝑋) + 𝑝(𝑋)exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋) =exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
𝑝(𝑋) =exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋) − 𝑝(𝑋)exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
𝑝(𝑋) =exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)(1 − 𝑝(𝑋))
𝑝(𝑋)
1 − 𝑝(𝑋)= exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)

Solution of Your Turn 4.3
Asymptotically no bias. The estimators are normally distributed: ̂𝛽(𝑝+1)×1∼ 𝑁𝑝+1( ̃𝛽, 𝑉 𝑎𝑟(̃𝛽)),
where ̂𝛽(𝑝+1)×1is the asymptotic ( 𝑛𝑜𝑏𝑠𝑒𝑟𝑣𝑎𝑡𝑖𝑜𝑛 → ∞ ) distribution of the parameters with the
estimated parameters ̃𝛽 as mean.

Exercise 4.1 – Logistic function
Using a little bit of algebra, prove that Equation ( 4.2) is equivalent to Equation ( 4.3). In other
words, the logistic function representation and logit representation for the logistic regression
model are equivalent.
Solution
𝑝(𝑋)
1 − 𝑝(𝑋)=
exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
1 + exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
1 − exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
1 + exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
=
exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
1 + exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
1 + exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋) −exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
1 + exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
= exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
1 + exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)(1 +exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋))
= exp(𝛽0 + 𝛽1𝑋)
Exercise 4.2 – Logistic regression
Suppose we collect data for a group of students in a statistics class with variables 𝑋1 =
hours studied, 𝑋2 = undergrad GPA, and 𝑌 = receive an A. We fit a logistic regression and
produce estimated coeﬀicients, ̂𝛽0 = −6, ̂𝛽1 = 0.05, ̂𝛽2 = 1.
• Estimate the probability that a student who studies for 40h and has an undergrad GPA
of 3.5 and gets an A in the class.
• How many hours would the student in the first part need to study to have a 50 % chance
of getting an A in the class?
Solution
Point 1. ̂𝛽0 = −6, ̂𝛽1 = 0.05, ̂𝛽2 = 1, 𝑋1 = 40, 𝑋2 = 3.5.
𝑝(𝑋1, 𝑋2) = exp( ̂𝛽0 + ̂𝛽1𝑋1 + ̂𝛽2𝑋2)
1 + exp( ̂𝛽0 + ̂𝛽1𝑋1 + ̂𝛽2𝑋2)
= exp(−6 + 0.05 × 40 + 1 × 3.5)
1 + exp(−6 + 0.05 × 40 + 1 × 3.5)
= 0.378
Point 2. We want 𝑋1 such that 𝑝(𝑋1, 𝑋2) = 0.5for 𝑋2 = 3.5.
100
4 Classification
exp( ̂𝛽0 + ̂𝛽1𝑋1 + ̂𝛽2𝑋2) = 𝑝(𝑋1, 𝑋2)
1 − 𝑃 (𝑋1, 𝑋2)= 0.5
1 − 0.5
⟺ exp( ̂𝛽0 + ̂𝛽1𝑋1 + ̂𝛽2𝑋2) = 1
⟺ ̂𝛽0 + ̂𝛽1𝑋1 + ̂𝛽2𝑋2 = log(1) = 0
⟺ −6 + 0.05𝑋1 + 𝑋2 = 0
⟺ 0.05𝑋1 = 6 − 𝑋2 = 6 − 3.5 = 2.5
⟺ 𝑋 1 = 50

Exercise 4.4 – Direction of the FTSE 100
In this question, we will use logistic regression to forecast the daily direction of the FTSE 100.
• Download the adjusted daily price for the FTSE 100 from 2014 to 2019.
• Compute the direction of the daily returns 𝑟dir, the macd, smi and adx .
• Shift the direction of daily returns by a day.
• Divide the data into a training set and a test set.
i) The test set is always the year 2019.
ii) For the training set, use the intervals 2015-2018, 2016-2018, 2017-2018 and the year 2018.
• Use glm() to fit a logistic regression model with 𝑟dir as the dependent variable and the
macd, smi, adx and the volume as the predictors for each training set.
• Use the right metric to compare the models. Which training set is the best? Why?

# Download FTSE100 data and remove NA
ticker <- "^FTSE"
ftse100 <- na.omit(getSymbols(ticker, auto.assign = FALSE,

4 Classification
to = "2019-12-31"))
# Adjusted daily returns
ftse100_rets <- Return.calculate(prices = ftse100, method = "log")[, 6]
# MACD
macd <- MACD(Cl(ftse100))[ , 2]
# SMI
smi <- SMI(HLC(ftse100))[, "SMI"]
# ADX
adx <- ADX(HLC(ftse100))[, "ADX"]
# Volume

## Shift returns by a day and convert to an xts object
ftse100_rets_lead_dat <- index(ftse100_rets)
ftse100_rets_lead_val <- as.vector(ftse100_rets)
ftse100_rets_lead <- xts(shift(ftse100_rets_lead_val, type = "lead"),
order.by = ftse100_rets_lead_dat)
direction_lead <- rep(0, length(ftse100_rets)) # Down
direction_lead[ftse100_rets_lead > 0] <- 1 # Up
df_ftse100 <- cbind(ftse100_rets_lead, direction_lead, macd, smi, adx, volume)
df_ftse100 <- na.omit(df_ftse100)
names(df_ftse100) <- c("adjclose_rets_lead", "direction_lead",
"macd", "smi", "adx", "volume")

5 Cross-validation
In this chapter, we discuss cross-validation. Cross-validation is a subset of a larger set of methods
known as resampling methods. Cross-validation can be used to estimate the test error associated
with a given statistical/machine learning method to evaluate its performance (model assessment),
or to select the appropriate level of flexibility (model selection or hyperparameter fitting).
Section 5.1 discusses the validation set approach. In Section 5.2, we present the leave-one-out
cross-validation technique. The K-fold cross-validation is presented in Section 5.3. We discuss
cross-validation for time series data in Section 5.5.
Learning objectives
At the end of this chapter, a learner should:
– Understand the difference between cross-validation and train/test sets approach.
– Understand various cross-validation methods for common data and time series.
– Feel at ease applying cross-validation to various problems.
Resources
This chapter is based on the following reference:
• An Introduction to Statistical Learning, Chapter 5 by Gareth James, Daniela Witten,
Trevor Hastie and Robert Tibshirani
5.1 The validation set approach
Suppose we want to find a set of variables that give the lowest test (not training!) MSE or error
rate. If we have a large data set, we can typically achieve this goal by (randomly) dividing the
data into two parts: a training set and a validation set (also called a hold-out set). This strategy
is called the validation set approach . We would then use the training set to fit each possible
model (the different combinations of variables) and use those models to predict the responses for
the observations in the validation set. The resulting validation MSE provides an estimate of the
test MSE. We will then choose the model with the lowest MSE when applied to the validation
data.
In finance, we usually split the data into two parts: in-sample and out-of-sample. The
former set is typically associated with the training set. For the latter, some people consider
it the validation set or the testing set . In what follows, we will use the in-sample set either
as the full training set , or split it into a training and a validation set. The idea is that (i)
models are fitted on the training set, (ii) the validation set is used to get an idea about the
108
5 Cross-validation
Figure. A set of 𝑛 observations are randomly split into a training set (shown in blue, containing
observations 7, 22, and 13, among others) and a validation set (shown in beige and containing
observation 91, among others). Source: James et al. (2013).
performance outside the training set, to choose a model, a set of hyperparameters, etc.,
and (iii) the testing set is really the one on which the performance is compared (i.e., there
is no more action here, only conclusion).
The statistical/machine learning method is fitted on the training set, and its performance is
evaluated on the validation set. We will illustrate the cross-validation approach by applying it
to linear and penalized regression models. This second model will be discussed later.
We use S&P 500 data for which we have computed lagged returns and technical indicators for
the period 2014-01-01 to 2019-12-31.
For demonstration purposes, we assume that the S&P 500 returns are independent once condi-
tioned on the explanatory variables (features).

We estimate the test MSE from the validation set MSE. We use here the simple approach of
regenerating several training and validation samples from the overall training set. 75% of the
2014-2018 weekly returns (randomly chosen) serves as the learning set, and the remaining 25%
forms the validation set. This is repeated 100 times to get 100 validation MSEs. We can compare
the MSE obtained from the validation set with the MSE on the test set (weekly returns in 2019).
We can compare both measures with a benchmark, for instance, the standard deviation of the
returns in the 2014-2018 period.
pos_train <- which(idx_train)

set.seed(2021)

for (i in 1:n_sim) {
pos_i <- sample(x = pos_train,
size = length(pos_train),
replace = FALSE)

Next, we will use the functions train() and trainControl() from the caret package ( Kuhn
(2024)) to carry out the (cross) validation.
In the function train() from the caret package, the argument preProcess allows us to carry
out some transformation on the predictors. A few methods, such as penalized linear regression,
are sensitive to the scale of the predictors. Here, we center each predictor and normalize it,
hereby obtaining for each predictor a set of observations with mean 0 and standard deviation 1
(for the training set).
The method argument of the train() function determines which machine learning method we
would like to use. The package glmnet package ( Friedman et al. (2025)) carries out penalized
regression (lasso, ridge, elastic net) and will be explored in the next chapter.
111
5 Cross-validation
Finally, the trControl argument determines what type of cross-validation is carried out. Here,
we set the method argument in trainControl() to "none". This instructs the function to carry
no cross-validation, which is the equivalent of fitting only on the training set.
We can revisit the former linear regression example with the function train().

While the validation set approach is conceptually simple and is easy to implement, it has two
potential drawbacks.
First, the validation estimate of the test MSE can be highly variable, depending on precisely
which observations are included in the training set and which observations are included in the
validation set. Second, only a subset of the observations (those that are included in the training
set) is used to fit the model. Since statistical/machine learning methods tend to perform worse
when trained on fewer observations, this suggests that the validation set MSE may tend to
overestimate the test MSE for the model fit on the entire data set.
The procedure to select the validation sets differs when we cannot assume that the data
are i.i.d. (such as time series). The leave-one-out cross-validation and the K-fold cross-
validation are designed for i.i.d. datasets. The expanding and sliding windows approaches
are for time series.
5.2 Leave-one-out cross-validation (LOOCV)
Leave-one-out cross-validation (LOOCV) is closely related to the validation set approach previ-
ously described, but it attempts to address that method’s drawbacks.
Like the validation set approach, LOOCV involves splitting the observations into two parts.
However, instead of creating two subsets of comparable size, a single observation (𝑥1, 𝑦1)is
used for the validation set, and the remaining observations {(𝑥2, 𝑦2), … , (𝑥𝑛, 𝑦𝑛)}make up the
training set. The statistical/machine learning method is fitted on the 𝑛−1 training observations,
113
5 Cross-validation
and a prediction ̂ 𝑦1 is made for the excluded observation, using its value 𝑥1. The MSE 1 is then
computed as (𝑦1 − ̂ 𝑦1)2.
Repeating this approach 𝑛 times in a way that the last subsets are {(𝑥1, 𝑦1), … , (𝑥𝑛−1, 𝑦𝑛−1)}for
the training set and (𝑥𝑛, 𝑦𝑛)for the validation test, leads to 𝑛 square errors MSE1, MSE2, … ,MSE𝑛.
The LOOCV validation MSE, which is an estimate for the test MSE, is the average of these 𝑛
error estimates:
𝐶𝑉(𝑛)= 1
𝑛
𝑛
∑
𝑖=1
MSE𝑖 .
Figure. A schematic diagram of LOOCV. A set of 𝑛 data points is repeatedly split into a training
set (shown in blue) containing all but one observation and a validation set that contains only
that observation (shown in beige). Source: James et al. (2013).
Here is an example with the lars regression. Here, we set the method argument in
trainControl() to "loocv". This instructs the function to carry out the LOOCV scheme.
hyp_grid <- expand.grid(alpha = 1,
lambda = 10^(seq(from = 0,
to = -6,
length.out = 20)))
model_plr_loocv <- train(form = fmla,
data = df_sp500[idx_train, ],
preProcess = c("center", "scale"),
method = "glmnet" ,
trControl = trainControl(method = "loocv"),
tuneGrid = hyp_grid)

LOOCV: pros and cons
LOOCV has a couple of major advantages over the traditional validation set:
• Less bias : the parameters are estimated with 𝑛 − 1 observations. Far more than in the
validation set approach. Consequently, the LOOCV approach tends not to overestimate
the test error rate as much as the validation set approach does.
115
5 Cross-validation
• Lower variability of MSE : contrarily to the validation set, performing LOOCV multiple
times will always yield the same results. There is no randomness in the training/validation
set splits since we separate 1 observation each time.
However, LOOCV can be expensive since the model has to be fitted 𝑛 times. This can be very
time-consuming if 𝑛 is large and if each individual model is slow to fit.
5.3 K-fold cross-validation
An alternative to LOOCV is k-fold CV . This approach involves randomly dividing the set of
observations into 𝑘 groups, or folds, of approximately equal size. The first fold is treated as a
validation set, and the model is fitted on the remaining 𝑘 − 1 folds. The mean squared error,
MSE1 is then computed on the observations in the held-out fold.
This procedure is repeated 𝑘 times; each time, a different fold is treated as a validation set. The
process results in 𝑘 estimates of the test error. The 𝑘-fold CV estimate is computed by averaging
these values:
𝐶𝑉(𝑘)= 1
𝑘
𝑘
∑
𝑖=1
MSE𝑖 .
Figure. A set of 𝑛 observations is randomly split into five non-overlapping groups. Each of these
fifths acts as a validation set (shown in beige) and the remainder as a training set (shown in
blue). Source: James et al. (2013).
The test error is estimated by averaging the five resulting MSE estimates.
model_plr_cv <- train(form = fmla,
data = df_sp500[idx_train, ],
preProcess = c("center", "scale"),
method = "glmnet" ,
trControl = trainControl(method = "cv",
number = 5),
tuneGrid = hyp_grid)

117
5 Cross-validation
K-fold cross-validation and LOOCV
LOOCV can be considered as a special case of 𝑘-fold CV in which 𝑘 is set equal to 𝑛. The most
obvious advantage of the 𝑘-fold CV approach compared to LOOCV is the lower computation
time. A less obvious but potentially more important advantage of 𝑘-fold CV is that it often
gives more accurate estimates of the test error rate than does LOOCV. This has to do with the
bias-variance trade-off.
From the perspective of bias reduction, it is clear that LOOCV is better than 𝑘-fold CV. The
training set contains more observations. However, it turns out that LOOCV has a higher variance
than does 𝑘-fold CV with 𝑘 < 𝑛. Why? The reason lies in the correlation between the models’
outputs.
When we perform LOOCV, we are, in effect, averaging the outputs (MSE) of 𝑛 fitted models
which have high (positive) correlation between one another, since they are trained on an almost
identical set of observations. In contrast, in case of a 𝑘-fold CV with 𝑘 < 𝑛 , the level of
correlation between the outputs is smaller. Since the mean of many highly correlated quantities
has a higher variance than does the mean of many quantities with lower correlation, the test
error estimate resulting from LOOCV tends to have higher variance than does the test error
estimate resulting from 𝑘-fold CV.
Performing a 𝑘-fold CV using 𝑘 = 5 or 𝑘 = 10 has been shown empirically to yield test MSE or
error rate estimates that suffer neither from excessively high bias nor from very high variance.
5.4 Cross-validation for classification problems
So far, we have illustrated the usage of cross-validation in the regression setting where the
outcome 𝑌 is quantitative, and so we have used MSE to quantify test error. But cross-validation
can also be a very useful approach in the classification setting when 𝑌 is qualitative. The only
difference is that we now use the number of misclassified observations rather than MSE. For
instance, the LOOCV error rate takes the form:
𝐶𝑉(𝑛)= 1
𝑛
𝑛
∑
𝑖=1
𝐸𝑟𝑟𝑖 ,
where 𝐸𝑟𝑟𝑖 = 𝐼(𝑦𝑖 ≠ ̂ 𝑦𝑖). The 𝑘-fold CV error rate and validation set error rates are defined
analogously.
5.5 Cross-validation for time series
Neither the cross-validation methods mentioned above nor the (random) validation set approach
are suitable for time series. This is due to the time order that is present in the time series. By
randomly selecting a cross-validation set, we might end up using future information to model
the past, which is problematic.
We will now show two approaches to carry out cross-validation on time series.
118
5 Cross-validation
Expanding window cross-validation
In this approach, there is a series of validation sets, each consisting of 𝑚 observations. The
corresponding training set consists only of observations that occurred before the observation
that forms the validation set. Hence, no future observations are used to train the model. For
each new CV fold, the previous validation set is added to the previous training set, and a new
(contiguous) validation set with 𝑚 observations is created. So the models are fitted on expanding
training sets, but the MSE for each validation is computed on a fixed size ( 𝑚) validation set.
The figure below illustrates the series of training and validation sets, where the black observations
form the training sets, and the yellow observations form the validation sets.
Figure. Expanding window CV. Source: Roy Yang (2018).
The package caret allows us to perform time-series CV easily.
model_plr_expanding <- train(form = fmla,
data = df_sp500[idx_train, ],
preProcess = c("center", "scale"),
method = "glmnet",
trControl = trainControl(method = "timeslice",
initialWindow = 104,
horizon = 52,
fixedWindow = FALSE,
allowParallel = TRUE),
tuneGrid = hyp_grid)

Here, we set the method argument in trainControl() to "timeslice". This instructs the
function to carry out time-series CV. The argument initialWindow (104) is the size of the
initial training set. The argument fixedWindow is set to FALSE to indicate we want an expanding
window. We set the argument horizon to 52 since we plan to use the same model to predict
returns for the next year (52 weeks).
120
5 Cross-validation
Sliding window cross-validation
As in the expanding window case, there is a series of validation sets, each consisting of 𝑚
observations. The corresponding training set consists only of observations that occurred before
the observation that forms the validation set. Hence, no future observations are used to train
the model.
For each new CV fold, the previous validation set is added to the previous training set, and a
new (contiguous) validation set with 𝑚 observations is created. However, we also discard the
𝑚 oldest observations from the training set. So the models are fitted on fixed-size training sets
where, at each CV fold, the oldest 𝑚 observations in the training sets are replaced with the 𝑚
observations from the validation set of the previous fold. The MSE for each validation fold is
computed on a fixed size ( 𝑚) test set.
The figure below illustrates the series of training and validation sets, where the black observations
form the training sets, the yellow observations from the validation sets and the gray observations
from the discarded sets.
Figure. Sliding window CV. Source: Roy Yang (2018).
model_plr_sliding <- train(form = fmla,
data = df_sp500[idx_train, ],
preProcess = c("center", "scale"),
method = "glmnet" ,
trControl = trainControl(method = "timeslice",
initialWindow = 104,
horizon = 52,
fixedWindow = TRUE,
allowParallel = TRUE),
tuneGrid = hyp_grid)
model_plr_sliding

## 4.832930e-01 0.01447882 NaN 0.01013593
## 1.000000e+00 0.01447882 NaN 0.01013593

df_sp500[idx_test, ])

5 Cross-validation
5.6 Solutions of Your Turns
Solution of Your Turn 5.1
kfolds <- 10
horizon <- 15
train_max_idx_fold <- floor((1:kfolds) * (n_train - horizon)/kfolds)
valid_max_idx_fold <- train_max_idx_fold + horizon
train_total <- df_sp500[idx_train, ] # All training data
mse_valid <- rep(0, 16)
# iteration over each fold
for (k in 1:kfolds) {
# Training and validation data for fold k
train_set_fold <- train_total[1:train_max_idx_fold[k], ]
valid_set_fold <- train_total[(train_max_idx_fold[k] + 1):valid_max_idx_fold[k], ]
# Training model on fold k
regfit_fold <- regsubsets(fmla,
data = train_set_fold,
method = "exhaustive",
nvmax = 16)
# Extract information (model matrix) for the validation set of fold k
valid_mat <- model.matrix(fmla, data = valid_set_fold)
# Validation fold MSE on each model
mse_valid_fold <- rep(NA, 16)
for (i in 1:16) {
coefi <- coef(regfit_fold, id = i)
pred <- valid_mat[, names(coefi)] %*% coefi
mse_valid_fold[i] <- mean((valid_set_fold[, "gspc_adjclose_lead"] - pred)^2)
}
# Sums the MSE of each fold
mse_valid <- mse_valid + mse_valid_fold
}
# Mean of the MSE of models on all folds
mse_valid <- mse_valid/16
# Select the best model in general across each fold (validation selection)
# mse_valid
# which.min(mse_valid)
coef(regfit_full, id = which.min(mse_valid))

The test error is the same for the three models. Given that the linear regression model has no
hyperparameters to tune, it is expected. The cross-validation model should, therefore, be the
same regardless of the method.
For the cross-validation error, we see that the fixed window performs worse than the expanding
window. This is most likely case-dependent as we might think of cases where past data is not
relevant for the present.
Surprisingly, the LOOCV method yields the smallest error. However, when performing linear
regression on lagged values of the output variable, it is expected, as this essentially decorrelates
the problem.

6 Linear model selection and regularization
The problem of selecting the appropriate predictors for statistical/machine learning methods
is at the heart of machine learning. A model is only as good as the data that is fed into it.
A complex model with poor predictors is likely to perform worse than a simpler model with
the appropriate predictors. We discuss in this chapter some ways in which the simple linear
model can be improved by replacing plain least squares fitting with some alternative fitting
procedures.
In this chapter, we discuss model selection in Section 6.1 and regularization techniques in Section
6.2. We end the chapter with the case study in Section 6.3.
Learning objectives
At the end of this chapter, a learner should:
– Understand model selection for linear regression (forward, backward, best).
– Understand penalized regression models.
– Apply linear models selection and penalized regression to trading.
Resources
This chapter is based on the following reference:
• An Introduction to Statistical Learning, Chapter 6 by Gareth James, Daniela Witten,
Trevor Hastie and Robert Tibshirani
6.1 Model selection
In previous chapters, we studied multiple linear regression:
𝑌 = 𝛽 0 + 𝛽1𝑋1 + 𝛽2𝑋2 + … + 𝛽𝑝𝑋𝑝 + 𝜖 ,
and used the least square approach to fit the model.
However, we might often want to use another fitting procedure instead of least squares. This is
because alternative fitting procedures can, at times, produce better prediction accuracy and/or
model interpretability. We will see many alternatives to least squares fitting.
130
6 Linear model selection and regularization
6.1.1 Best subset selection
To perform best subset selection , we fit a separate least squares regression for each possible
combination of the 𝑝 predictors. We then look at all of the resulting models with the goal of
identifying the best one.
We carry out the selection process using the following algorithm:
i. Let ℳ0 denote the null model containing no predictors. This model simply predicts the
sample mean for each observation.
ii. For 𝑘 = 1, 2, … , 𝑝:
a) Fit all (𝑝
𝑘)models that contain exactly 𝑘 predictors.
b) Pick the best among these (𝑝
𝑘)models and call it ℳ𝑘. Here, best is defined as having the
smallest residual sum of squares (RSS) or, equivalently, the largest 𝑅2.
iii. Select a single best model from among ℳ0, … , ℳ𝑝 using cross-validated prediction error,
AIC, BIC, or adjusted 𝑅2.
The point iii. identifies the best out of 𝑝 + 1 models. This task must be performed with
care because the RSS of these 𝑝 + 1 models decreases monotonically, and the 𝑅2 increases
monotonically. This will result in choosing always the model with the largest number of variables.
Hence, we should use selection criteria (such as cross-validated prediction error, AIC, BIC, or
adjusted 𝑅2) that are not affected by the number of variables included in the model.
Specifically, we will choose the model with the lowest BIC, AIC, or cross validated prediction
error; or the largest adjusted 𝑅2.
Some comments
The same principles apply to other models, such as logistic regression. In the case of logistic
regression, instead of ordering models by RSS as described in step ii. of the algorithm previously
described, we use the deviance, a measure that plays the role of RSS for a broader class of models.
The deviance is negative two times the maximized log-likelihood; the smaller the deviance, the
better the fit. While best subset selection is a simple and conceptually appealing approach, it
suffers from computational limitations . Indeed, the number of possible models that must be
considered grows rapidly as 𝑝 increases. The stepwise selection method is a computationally
eﬀicient alternative to best subset selection.
6.1.2 Stepwise selection
Stepwise methods, which explore a far more restricted set of models, are attractive alternatives
to best subset selection. There are two stepwise selection methods:
6.1.2.1 Forward stepwise selection
It begins with a model containing no predictors, and then it adds predictors to the model, one
at a time, until all of the predictors are in the model. In particular, at each step the variable
that gives the greatest additional improvement to the fit is added to the model. The algorithm
is the following:
i. Let ℳ0 denote the null model containing no predictors.
131
6 Linear model selection and regularization
ii. For 𝑘 = 0, … , 𝑝 − 1:
a) Consider all 𝑝 − 𝑘 models that augment the predictors in ℳ𝑘 with one additional
predictor.
b) Choose the best (highest 𝑅2 or smallest RSS) among these 𝑝 − 𝑘models call it ℳ𝑘+1
iii. Select a single best model from among ℳ0, … , ℳ𝑝 using cross-validated prediction error,
AIC, BIC, or adjusted 𝑅2.
Unlike best subset selection, which involves fitting 2𝑝 models, forward stepwise selection involves
fitting one null model, along with 𝑝 − 𝑘 models in the 𝑘th iteration, for 𝑘 = 0, … , 𝑝 − 1. This
amounts to a total of 1 + ∑
𝑝−1
𝑘=0(𝑝 − 𝑘) = 1 + 𝑝(𝑝 + 1)/2. This is a substantial difference: when
𝑝 = 20 , the best subset selection requires fitting 1,048,576 models, whereas forward stepwise
selection requires fitting only 211 models.
Forward stepwise selection’s computational advantage over best subset selection is clear. Even
though forward stepwise tends to do well in practice, it is not guaranteed to find the best possible
model out of all 2𝑝 models containing subsets of the 𝑝 predictors.
For instance, suppose that in a given data set with 𝑝 = 3, the best possible one-variable model
contains 𝑋1, and the best possible two-variables model instead contains 𝑋2 and 𝑋3. Then
forward stepwise selection will fail to select the best possible two-variable model, because ℳ1
will contain 𝑋1, so ℳ2 must also contain ℳ1 together with one additional variable.
6.1.2.2 Backward stepwise selection
Backward stepwise selection, unlike forward stepwise selection, begins with the full least squares
model containing all 𝑝 predictors, and then iteratively removes the least useful predictor, one at
a time. Details are given in the following algorithm:
i. Let ℳ𝑝 denote the full model, which contains all 𝑝 predictors.
ii. For 𝑘 = 𝑝, 𝑝 − 1, … , 1:
a) Consider all 𝑘 models that contain all but one of the predictors in ℳ𝑘, for a total of
𝑘 − 1 predictors
b) Chose the best (highest 𝑅2 or smallest RSS) among these 𝑘 models and call it ℳ𝑘−1
iii. Select a single best model from among ℳ0, … , ℳ𝑝 using cross-validated prediction error,
AIC, BIC, or adjusted 𝑅2.
Like forward stepwise selection, the backward selection approach searches through only 1+𝑝(𝑝 +
1)/2models, and so can be applied in settings where 𝑝 is too large to use best subset selection.
Like forward stepwise selection, it is not guaranteed that backward stepwise selection yields the
best model.
Backward selection requires 𝑛 > 𝑝 to fit the full model. In contrast, forward stepwise can be
used when 𝑛 < 𝑝 and so is the only viable subset method when 𝑝 is very large .
Choosing the optimal model
We saw before that 𝑅2 and RSS are not good indicators for choosing the best model since they
are affected by the number of variables included. We are looking for the best model with respect
to the test error (not the training error!). Hence, we need to estimate such an error. There are
two common approaches:
132
6 Linear model selection and regularization
a) We can indirectly estimate test error by making an adjustment to the training error to
account for the bias due to overfitting by using AIC, BIC, or adjusted 𝑅2.
b) We can directly estimate the test error using either a validation set approach or a cross-
validation approach.
Adjusting the training error rate
The following indicators allow us to adjust the training error MSE (recall that 𝑀 𝑆𝐸 = 𝑅𝑆𝑆/𝑛)
such that we can compare models with different numbers of variables.
• Akaike Information Criterion, 𝐴𝐼𝐶 = 1
𝑛 ̂ 𝜎2 (𝑅𝑆𝑆 + 2𝑑 ̂ 𝜎2), where ̂ 𝜎2 is an estimate of the
error associated with each response measurement, and 𝑑 indicates the number of predictors.
• Bayesian Information Criterion, 𝐵𝐼𝐶 = 1
𝑛 ̂ 𝜎2 (𝑅𝑆𝑆 +log(𝑛)𝑑 ̂ 𝜎2).
• Adjusted 𝑅2, adj 𝑅2 = 1 − 𝑅𝑆𝑆/(𝑛−𝑑−1)
𝑇 𝑆𝑆/(𝑛−1), where 𝑇 𝑆𝑆 = ∑(𝑦𝑖 − ̄ 𝑦)2.
A large value of Adjusted 𝑅2 is equivalent to minimizing 𝑅𝑆𝑆/(𝑛 − 𝑑 − 1). Notice that BIC
replaces the 2𝑑 ̂ 𝜎2 by log(𝑛)𝑑 ̂ 𝜎2 term, where 𝑛 is the number of observations. Since log (𝑛) > 2for
any 𝑛 > 7, the BIC statistic generally places a heavier penalty on models with many variables
and hence results in the selection of smaller models
Estimating the test error rate
As an alternative to the approaches just discussed, we can directly estimate the test error using
the validation set and cross-validation methods (CV) discussed in the previous chapter. The
main advantage of using this procedure relative to AIC, BIC, … is that it provides a direct
estimate of the test error, and makes fewer assumptions about the true underlying model.
Illustration
Here, we apply the best subset selection approach to the df_sp500 data set.
library("here")

6.2 Regularization methods
6.2.1 Ridge regression
The least squares fitting procedure estimates 𝛽0, 𝛽1, … , 𝛽𝑝 using the values that minimize:
𝑅𝑆𝑆 =
𝑛
∑
𝑖=1
(𝑦𝑖 − 𝛽0 −
𝑝
∑
𝑗=1
𝛽𝑗𝑥𝑖𝑗)
2
.
Ridge regression is similar to the least squares, but we minimize a different quantity here. The
ridge regression coeﬀicient estimates ̂𝛽𝑅 are the values that minimize:
𝑅𝑆𝑆 + 𝜆
𝑝
∑
𝑗=1
𝛽2
𝑗 , (6.1)
where 𝜆 ≥ 0 is a tuning parameter to be determined separately.
As with least squares, ridge regression seeks coeﬀicient estimates that fit the data well by making
the RSS small. The second term, 𝜆 ∑
𝑝
𝑗=1 𝛽2
𝑗 , called a shrinkage penalty, is small when 𝛽1, … , 𝛽𝑝
are close to zero, and so it has the effect of shrinking the estimates of 𝛽𝑗 towards zero. The
tuning parameter 𝜆 serves to control the relative impact of these two terms on the regression
coeﬀicient estimates. When 𝜆 = 0 , the penalty term has no effect, and ridge regression will
140
6 Linear model selection and regularization
produce the least squares estimates. However, as 𝜆 → ∞ , the impact of the shrinkage penalty
grows, and the ridge regression coeﬀicient estimates will approach zero.
Linear regression may encounter problems in which the model’s estimated coeﬀicients can be-
come relatively large, making the model so unstable that it becomes sensitive to the inputs.
Which relates to the problems with a few observations or variables. Ridge regression allows for
regularizing this. Ridge regression’s advantage over least squares is rooted in the bias-variance
trade-off. As 𝜆 increases, the flexibility of the ridge regression fit decreases, leading to decreased
variance but increased bias.
It should be noted that ridge regression will produce a different set of coeﬀicient estimates,
̂𝛽𝑅
𝜆 for each value of 𝜆. Therefore, selecting a good value of 𝜆 is important. 𝜆 is known as a
hyperparameter and is usually estimated through cross-validation.
The shrinkage penalty is applied to 𝛽1, … , 𝛽𝑝 but not 𝛽0 (the intercept) in Equation ( 6.1). We
want to shrink the estimated association of each variable with the response; however, we do not
want to shrink the intercept, which is simply a measure of the mean value of the response when
𝑥𝑖1 = 𝑥𝑖2 = … = 𝑥 𝑖𝑝 = 0.
The standard least squares coeﬀicient estimates are scale equivariant , which means mul-
tiplying 𝑋𝑗 by a constant 𝑐 simply leads to scaling the least squares coeﬀicient estimates
by a factor of 1/𝑐. In other words, regardless of how the 𝑗th predictor is scaled, 𝑋𝑗 ̂𝛽𝑗
will remain the same. In contrast, the ridge regression coeﬀicient estimates can change
substantially when multiplying a given predictor by a constant. In other words, 𝑋𝑗 ̂𝛽𝑅
𝑗,𝜆
will depend not only on the value of 𝜆, but also on the scaling of the 𝑗th predictor. In fact,
the value of 𝑋𝑗 ̂𝛽𝑅
𝑗,𝜆 may even depend on the scaling of the other predictors! Therefore, it
is best to apply ridge regression after standardizing the predictors using the formula:
̃ 𝑥𝑖𝑗 = 𝑥𝑖𝑗
√ 1
𝑛 ∑
𝑛
𝑖=1(𝑥𝑖𝑗 − ̄ 𝑥𝑖𝑗)2
so that they are all on the same scale. In the equation above the denominator is the
estimated standard deviation of the 𝑗th predictor. Consequently, all of the standardized
predictors will have a standard deviation of one.
Let us illustrate the use of ridge regression with the same data set and model as before. We use
the package caret.
library("caret")

initialWindow = 104,
horizon = 52,
skip = 52,
fixedWindow = TRUE,
allowParallel = TRUE)
grid_ridge <- expand.grid(alpha = 0,
lambda = seq(from = 0,

length.out = 1001))

model_ridge_best <- train(form = fmla,
data = df_sp500[idx_train,],
method = "glmnet",
tuneGrid = grid_ridge,
trControl = ctr_train,
preProc = c("center", "scale"),
metric = "Rsquared")
We choose R-squared as the metric to select the best model here. For models with small
correlation between predictors and dependent variables, penalized regression (lasso, ridge,
elastic net) will set most (all for lasso) the coeﬀicients to 0 and hence return the mean of
the dependent variable. The cv-RMSE, in that case, is not a good way to select the best
model.
We can find the optimal value of the penalization parameter 𝜆 as follows:

6.2.2 Lasso regression
Ridge regression does have one obvious disadvantage. Unlike best subset, forward stepwise, and
backward stepwise selection, which will generally select models that involve just a subset of the
variables, ridge regression will include all 𝑝 predictors in the final model. The penalty 𝜆 ∑ 𝛽2
𝑗
in Equation ( 6.1) will shrink all of the coeﬀicients towards zero, but it will not set any of them
exactly to zero (unless 𝜆 = ∞). This may not be a problem for prediction accuracy, but it can
create a challenge in model interpretation in settings in which the number of variables 𝑝 is quite
large.
The lasso is an alternative to ridge regression that overcomes this disadvantage. The lasso
coeﬀicients, ̂𝛽𝐿
𝜆 , minimize the quantity:
𝑅𝑆𝑆 + 𝜆
𝑝
∑
𝑗=1
|𝛽𝑗| =
𝑛
∑
𝑖=1
(𝑦𝑖 − 𝛽0 −
𝑝
∑
𝑗=1
𝛽𝑗𝑥𝑖𝑗)
2
+ 𝜆
𝑝
∑
𝑗=1
|𝛽𝑗| , (6.2)
Comparing Equation ( 6.2) to Equation ( 6.1), we see that the lasso and ridge regression have
similar formulations. The only difference is that the 𝛽2
𝑗 term in the ridge regression penalty ( 6.1)
has been replaced by |𝛽𝑗| in the lasso penalty ( 6.2). In other words, the lasso uses an 𝐿1 penalty
instead of an 𝐿2 penalty. The 𝐿1 norm of a coeﬀicient vector 𝛽 is given by ||𝛽||1 = ∑ |𝛽𝑗|.
As with ridge regression, the lasso shrinks the coeﬀicient estimates towards zero. However, in
the case of the lasso, the 𝐿1 penalty forces some of the coeﬀicient estimates to be exactly equal
to zero when the tuning parameter 𝜆 is suﬀiciently large. Hence, much like best subset selection,
the lasso performs variable selection. As a result, models generated from the lasso are generally
much easier to interpret than those produced by ridge regression. We say that the lasso yields
sparse models, that is, models that involve only a subset of the variables. As in ridge regression,
selecting a good value of 𝜆 for the lasso is critical.
To understand the difference between ridge regression and lasso regression, imagine a situation
with identical predictors. Ridge regression shrinks the coeﬀicients of correlated predictors to-
wards each other (identical predictors would get identical coeﬀicients), while lasso will tend to
pick one and ignore the rest.
143
6 Linear model selection and regularization

6.2.3 Elastic net
A generalization of the lasso and ridge model is the elastic net (Zou and Hastie , 2005). This
model combines the two types of penalties:
𝑅𝑆𝑆 + 𝜆1
𝑝
∑
𝑗=1
𝛽2
𝑗 + 𝜆2
𝑝
∑
𝑗=1
|𝛽𝑗| . (6.3)
The advantage of this model is that it enables effective regularization via the ridge-type penalty
with the feature selection quality of the lasso penalty. Zou and Hastie (2005) suggest that this
model will more effectively deal with groups of high correlated predictors. Both penalties require
tuning to achieve optimal performance.
Equation ( 6.3) is equivalent to solving the following problem:
min
𝛽0 ,𝛽
1
𝑁
𝑁
∑
𝑖=1
𝑙(𝑦𝑖, 𝛽0 + 𝛽′𝑥𝑖) + 𝜆 [1
2(1 − 𝛼)||𝛽||2
2 + 𝛼||𝛽||1] ,
over a grid of values of 𝜆 covering the entire range. Here 𝑙(𝑦, 𝜂)is the negative log-likelihood
contribution for observation 𝑖. The elastic-net penalty is controlled by 𝛼, and bridges the gap
between lasso ( 𝛼 = 1, the default) and ridge 𝛼 = 0. The tuning parameter 𝜆 controls the overall
strength of the penalty ( Simon et al. , 2011).

6.3 Case study: Stock picking Nasdaq using lasso regression
library("doBy")
library("PerformanceAnalytics")
library("xts")
load(file = here("data", "nasdaq20.rda"))
n_stocks <- length(top_nasdaq)
model_list <- sapply(top_nasdaq, function(x) NULL)
idx_train <- stock_ind$AAPL$date >= "2015-01-01" & stock_ind$AAPL$date < "2019-01-01"
idx_test <- stock_ind$AAPL$date >= "2019-01-01" & stock_ind$AAPL$date < "2019-12-01"
ctr_train <- trainControl(method = "timeslice",
initialWindow = 104,
horizon = 52,
fixedWindow = TRUE,
allowParallel = TRUE)
grid_lasso <- expand.grid(alpha = 1,
lambda = seq(from = 0,
to = 0.05,
length.out = 26))
for (i in 1:n_stocks) {
base_name <- tolower(gsub("[[:punct:]]", "", top_nasdaq[i]))
fmla <- paste0(paste0(base_name,"_adjclose_lead"), " ~ . " ,
"-date -direction_lead" )
fmla <- as.formula(fmla)

data = stock_ind[[top_nasdaq[i]]][idx_train, ],
method = "glmnet",
tuneGrid = grid_lasso,
trControl = ctr_train,

Exercise 6.2 – Ten-fold cross-validation for best subset model selection
Revisit the subset selection illustration but determine the best model using ten-fold cross-
validation.
Solution
pos_train <- which(idx_train)
K <- 10
set.seed(1234)
folds <- sample(1:K, n_train, replace = TRUE)
fmla <- gspc_adjclose_lead ~ . -date -direction_lead
cv_mse <- matrix(data = NA, nrow = K, ncol = 16)
dimnames(cv_mse) <- list(1:K, 1:16)
for (k in 1:K) {
train_k <- pos_train[folds != k]
valid_k <- pos_train[folds == k]
regfit <- regsubsets(fmla,
data = df_sp500[train_k, ],
method = "exhaustive",
nvmax = 16)
for (i in 1:16) {
mat <- model.matrix(fmla, df_sp500[valid_k, ])
coefi <- coef(regfit, id = i)
xvars <- names(coefi)
pred <- mat[,xvars] %*% coefi
cv_mse[k,i] <- mean((df_sp500[valid_k, "gspc_adjclose_lead"] - pred)^2)
}
}
cv_mse <- apply(cv_mse, 2, mean)
which.min(cv_mse)
regfit_full <- regsubsets(fmla,

7 Tree-based methods
This chapter is about tree-based methods for regression and classification. Tree-based methods
are simple and useful for interpretation and are thoroughly discussed in Section 7.1. However,
they usually are not as successful as supervised learning approaches, such as penalized regression
when it comes to prediction accuracy.
Therefore, we also introduce bagging and random forests in Section 7.2. Each approach involves
producing multiple trees, which are combined to yield a single consensus prediction. We will see
that combining a large number of trees can often result in dramatic improvements in prediction
accuracy at the expense of some loss in interpretation. We end the chapter with the case study
in Section 7.3.
Learning objectives
At the end of this chapter, a learner should:
– Understand the principle of tree methods.
– Understand how tree methods can be extended to produce bagging and random forest
algorithms.
– Apply tree/bagging/random forest to trading.
Resources
This chapter is based on the following reference:
• An Introduction to Statistical Learning, Chapter 8 by Gareth James, Daniela Witten,
Trevor Hastie and Robert Tibshirani
7.1 Decision trees
7.1.1 An overview of tree-based methods
In this section, we describe tree-based methods for regression and classification. These involve
stratifying or segmenting the predictor space into a number of simple regions. Then, to make
a prediction for a given observation, we typically use the mean or the mode of the training
observations in the region to which it belongs. Since the set of splitting rules used to segment
the predictor space can be summarized in a tree, these approaches are known as decision tree
methods.
Tree-based methods are simple and useful for interpretation. However, they typically are not as
competitive as other supervised learning methods in terms of prediction accuracy.
157
7 Tree-based methods
Bagging and random forests are approaches that involve generating multiple trees, which are
then combined to yield a single consensus prediction.
7.1.2 Regression trees - An example
To give intuition, we start with a simple example. Let us consider the data frame df_sp500,
which we have encountered in the previous chapters. It contains the lead weekly returns and
direction of the S&P 500, a few technical indicators, and lagged returns of orders 0 to 3.
We wish to predict the returns for next week based on the current returns and the technical
indicator atr. A regression tree fit for this data consists of a series of splitting rules, starting at
the top of the tree, as shown in the figure below.
atr >= 59
atr < 49
gspc_adjclose_lag0 >= -0.0077

The top split assigns observations having atr >= 59 to the left branch. The predicted response
for these observations is given by the mean response value for the returns in the dataset with
atr >= 59 , that is −0.016 and represents 3% of the observations. Observations with atr <
59 are assigned to the right branch, and then that group is further subdivided by atr then by
gspc_adjclose_lag0 (current adjusted close returns).
Overall, the tree stratifies or segments the returns into four regions of predictor space: obser-
vations with atr >= 59 (𝑅1), observations with atr < 49 and adjusted close larger or equal
than -0.77 % (𝑅2), observations with atr < 49 and adjusted close lower than -0.77 % (𝑅3)and
observations with 49 <= atr < 59 (𝑅4).
rpart.rules(trees, cover = TRUE, roundint = FALSE)
## gspc_adjclose_lead cover
## -0.01632 when atr >= 59 3%
## -0.00066 when atr < 49 & gspc_adjclose_lag0 >= -0.0077 74%
158
7 Tree-based methods
## 0.00712 when atr < 49 & gspc_adjclose_lag0 < -0.0077 17%
## 0.01635 when atr is 49 to 59 5%
We might interpret the previous regression tree as follows:
• atr is the most important factor in determining the next returns, and weeks with atr >=
59 yield a return of -1.6%. Similarly, if atr is between 49 and 59, the returns forecast is
1.6%. So, given an atr is greater than 49, the current returns play no role in the forecast
of the next returns.
• For atr < 49 , the current returns determine if the future returns will be -0.066% (for
gspc_adjclose_lag0 >= -0.0077 ) or 0.71% (for gspc_adjclose_lag0 < -0.0077 ).
The regression tree just shown is likely an over-simplification of the true relationship between
gspc_adjclose_lead, gspc_adjclose_lag0, and atr. However, it has advantages over other
regression models: it is easier to interpret, and it has a nice graphical representation .
7.1.3 Regression trees – Some definitions
The regions 𝑅1, 𝑅2, 𝑅3 and 𝑅4 are known as terminal nodes or leaves of the tree. Decision trees
are typically drawn upside down. That is, leaves are at the bottom of the tree. The points
along the tree where the predictor space is split are referred to as internal nodes (in the previous
example, nodes are indicated by atr >= 59 , atr < 49 and gspc_adjclose_lag0 >= -0.0077 ).
We refer to the segments of the trees that connect the nodes as branches.
7.1.4 Stratification of the feature space
To build a regression tree, there are basically two steps:
1. We divide the predictor space (i.e., all the possible values for 𝑋1, 𝑋2, … , 𝑋𝑝 into distinct
and non-overlapping regions, say 𝑅1, 𝑅2, … , 𝑅𝑝
2. Then for every observation that falls in a particular region 𝑅𝑗, with 𝑗 = 1, … , 𝑝, we make
the same prediction, which is simply the mean of the response values of the training
observations in 𝑅𝑗.
But how do we construct the regions 𝑅1, 𝑅2, … , 𝑅𝑝?
We take a top-down approach known as binary splitting . We start by selecting a predictor 𝑋𝑗
and a cutpoint 𝑠 such that splitting the predictor space into the regions {𝑋 ∣ 𝑋 𝑗 < 𝑠} and
{𝑋 ∣ 𝑋𝑗 ≥ 𝑠} leads to the greatest possible reduction in RSS.
This can be written as follows:
𝑅1(𝑗, 𝑠) = {𝑋 ∣ 𝑋𝑗 < 𝑠} and 𝑅2(𝑗, 𝑠) = {𝑋 ∣ 𝑋𝑗 ≥ 𝑠} ,
and we seek the value of 𝑗 and 𝑠 that minimize the equation:
∑
𝑖∶𝑥𝑖 ∈𝑅1 (𝑗,𝑠)
(𝑦𝑖 − ̂ 𝑦𝑅1
)2 + ∑
𝑖∶𝑥𝑖 ∈𝑅2 (𝑗,𝑠)
(𝑦𝑖 − ̂ 𝑦𝑅2
)2 ,
159
7 Tree-based methods
where ̂ 𝑦𝑅1
is the mean response for the training observations in 𝑅1(𝑗, 𝑠), and ̂ 𝑦𝑅2
is the mean
response for the training observations in 𝑅2(𝑗, 𝑠).
Finding the values of 𝑗 and 𝑠 that minimize the equation above can be done quite quickly,
especially when the number of features 𝑝 is not too large. Next, we repeat the process, looking
for the best predictor and best cut-point to split the data further to minimize the RSS within
each of the resulting regions.
However, this time, instead of splitting the entire predictor space, we split one of the two
previously identified regions. The process continues until a stopping criterion is reached; for
instance, we may continue until no region contains more than five observations. Once step 1 is
completed, we can easily implement step 2 and make our predictions. A five-region example of
this approach is shown below.
Top Left: A partition of two-dimensional feature space that could not result from recursive
binary splitting. Top Right: The output of recursive binary splitting on a two-dimensional
example. Bottom Left: A tree corresponding to the partition in the top right panel. Bottom
Right: A perspective plot of the prediction surface corresponding to that tree. Source: James
et al. (2013).
160
7 Tree-based methods
7.1.5 Tree pruning
The process just described may produce good predictions on the training set, but is likely to
overfit the data, leading to poor test set performance. This is because the resulting tree might
be too complex (i.e., too many regions).
One alternative approach is to prune the tree : we obtain a very large tree 𝑇0 and then prune it
back to obtain a subtree. Our goal is to select a subtree that leads to the lowest test error. Since
estimating the CV error for every possible subtree would be cumbersome, we can consider the
cost complexity pruning , also known as weakest link pruning .
Tree pruning - Cost complexity pruning
Rather than considering every possible subtree, we consider a sequence of trees indexed by a
nonnegative tuning parameter 𝛼. For each value of 𝛼, there corresponds a subtree 𝑇 ⊂ 𝑇 0 such
that ∑
∣𝑇 ∣
𝑚=1 ∑𝑖∶𝑥𝑖 ∈𝑅𝑚
(𝑦𝑖 − ̂ 𝑦𝑅𝑚
)2 + 𝛼|𝑇 |, is as small as possible. ∣ 𝑇 ∣ indicates the number of
terminal nodes of the tree 𝑇, 𝑅𝑚 is the subset of the predictor space corresponding to the 𝑚th
terminal node, and ̂ 𝑦𝑅𝑚
is the predicted response associated with 𝑅𝑚.
The tuning parameter 𝛼 controls the trade-off between the subtree’s complexity and its fit to
the training data.
With 𝛼 = 0, the subtree 𝑇 will simply equal 𝑇0. As 𝛼 increases, there is a price to pay for having
a tree with many terminal nodes, and so the quantity above will tend to be minimized for a
smaller subtree. We can select a value of 𝛼 using a validation set or cross-validation. We then
return to the full data set and obtain the subtree corresponding to the 𝛼 value just found.
To summarize, we use recursive binary splitting to grow a large tree on the training data. We
apply cost complexity pruning to the large tree to obtain a sequence of best subtrees. We
estimate the right 𝛼 and consider the subtree associated to it.
7.1.6 Fitting a regression tree
Below, we will use the package rpart (Therneau and Atkinson (2025)) for building the regression
and classification trees. The package rpart.plot (Milborrow (2025)) is used to display the
tree.
We have already defined a training set. We will fit the tree to the training data (all the predic-
tors/columns except date and direction_lead). Our response variable, as usual, is the weekly
return in gspc_adjclose_lead.
We use the function rpart() to fit a tree. The syntax of this function is quite similar to that of
the lm() function.
library("rpart")
fmla_trees <- gspc_adjclose_lead ~ . -date - direction_lead
# ?rpart::rpart()
model_trees <- rpart(fmla_trees,
data = df_sp500[idx_train, ])
names(model_trees)

7.1.7 Classification trees
Classification trees – Characteristics
A classification tree is very similar to a regression tree, except it is used to predict a qualitative
response rather than a quantitative one. We predict that each observation belongs to the most
commonly occurring class of training observations in the region to which it belongs.
165
7 Tree-based methods
The classification tree is grown (i.e., the splits are chosen) in the same way as the regression tree
(that is, we use recursive binary splitting). However, in the classification setting, RSS cannot be
used as a criterion for making the binary splits.
Alternatives to RSS for the classification tree are represented by the classification error rate, the
Gini index , or the entropy. These indicators are described below.
Classification trees – Useful indicators
• The classification error rate is simply the fraction of the training observations in a certain
region that do not belong to the most common class:
𝐸𝑚 = 1 − max
𝑘
( ̂ 𝑝𝑚𝑘) ,
where ̂ 𝑝𝑚𝑘 represents the proportion of training observations in the 𝑚th region that belong
to the 𝑘th class. However, it turns out that classification error is not suﬀiciently sensitive for
tree-growing, and in practice, two other measures are preferable.
• The Gini index is defined by:
𝐺𝑚 =
𝐾
∑
𝑘=1
̂ 𝑝𝑚𝑘(1 − ̂ 𝑝𝑚𝑘) ,
a measure of total variance across the 𝐾 classes. This index takes on a small value if all of the
̂ 𝑝𝑚𝑘’s are close to zero or one. For this reason, the Gini index is referred to as a measure of node
purity: A small value indicates that a node contains predominantly observations from a single
class.
• An alternative to the Gini index is entropy, given by:
𝐷𝑚 = −
𝐾
∑
𝑘=1
̂ 𝑝𝑚𝑘 log ̂ 𝑝𝑚𝑘 .
Since 0 ≤ ̂ 𝑝𝑚𝑘 ≤ 1, it follows that 0 ≤ − ̂ 𝑝𝑚𝑘 log ̂ 𝑝𝑚𝑘. One can show that the entropy will take
on a value near zero if the ̂ 𝑝𝑚𝑘’s are all near zero or near one. Therefore, like the Gini index,
the entropy will take on a small value if the 𝑚th node is pure.
Either the Gini index or the entropy are typically used to evaluate the quality of a particular
split, since these two approaches are more sensitive to node purity than is the classification error
rate. Any of these three approaches might be used when pruning the tree, but the classification
error rate is preferable if prediction accuracy of the final pruned tree is the goal .
Entropy (for data science) Clearly Explained!!!, StatQuest
166
